msgstr ""
msgstr "The small town of Asti in Northern Italy attracted the attention of antiquity lovers already in the last century. In the middle of the century, it belonged to the most powerful republics of Northern Italy. For many years it was the residence of a bishop, which left a characteristic mark on the city: the 16th-century Gothic cathedral stood almost untouched by time, and nearby was the 13th-century church of San Secondo. The city had many palaces and 30 watchtowers were preserved."
msgstr "In early May 1872, the manager of the Kamenets-Podolsk Treasury Chamber, S.N. Tsvet, and his wife arrived in Asti, having decided to explore the sights of this city on their way from Genoa to Switzerland."
msgstr "In the early 70s of the last century, Asti had a little over 30 thousand inhabitants. With two crowded fairs and silk factories, it conducted significant trade in woolen goods, leather, hats, and truffles. The city's surroundings were famous for highly valued wines, among which the sparkling muscat was particularly well-known. Tourists loved to visit here, the birthplace of the famous Italian poet Vittorio Alfieri (1749—1803), the creator of classical tragedy in Italy, who passionately advocated for the rights of the oppressed people and for the unification of fragmented Italy. The residents of Asti still carefully preserve the house where their remarkable compatriot was born and lived. Even in the middle of the last century, one of the city squares was named after him, and in 1862 a monument to Alfieri was erected on it. It was onto this square that the windows of the \"Reale\" hotel faced, where the legendary G. Garibaldi once stayed, and where the Tsvet spouses settled."
msgstr "Here, on May 14[^26.1], 1872, Mikhail Tsvet was born."
msgstr "Five days later, this fact was recorded in the city magistrate with the following entry: \"On May 19, 1872, the physician Fontaine Luigi, who delivered the baby, stated that at half past eleven in the morning on the fourteenth of the current month, Signora de Dorozza Maria, daughter of the currently living Signor Nicolo, twenty-six years old, born in Kütahya, the center of the province (Turkey), wife of the Knight of the Order of St. Anna, State Councilor Signor de Zevet Simon, son of the deceased Nicolo, forty-two years old, chairman of the Podolsk Treasury Chamber, born in Chernigov (Little Russia), passing through this city, residing with him, gave birth in this city in the 'Reale' hotel, located on Alfieri Square, to a male child, who due to poor health was not brought to this institution. Based on the presented medical certificate and after personally verifying the fact of birth and the sex of the child, he was given the name Michele, the authenticity of which, after reading this act, I and the other attendees confirm with our signatures\" \\[1\\], followed by the signatures of the witnesses — physician F. Luigi, notary G. Perfumo, and two Italian officials — B. Giovanni and N. Grandi (see also Debenedetti E., 1956). Thus, far from the land of his ancestors, the birth of Mikhail Semyonovich Tsvet was recorded as Michele, son of Simon de Zevet (see fig. on p. 27)."
msgstr "Among the first to familiarize themselves with this document were Hesse and Weil, who tried to find more complete information about Tsvet's parents. As a result, they stated: \"Since nothing more is known about M. Tsvet's mother later (i.e., after his birth — E.S.), it is understandable that various guesses are made about Tsvet's origins... there is an assumption that M. Tsvet's father, born in 1830, but whose birthplace is unknown to us, or his grandfather Nikolai were in Switzerland, and that one or the other emigrated from Russia\" (Hesse G., Weil H., 1954, S.31). As arguments for this version, the authors cited the fact that in the Bernese region of Switzerland, as well as in Germany, the surname Zwez is quite widespread, which could have been adopted with a slight modification by the Tsvets after their emigration from Russia. Noting the limited information about the Tsvet family, Hesse and Weil expressed hope that such information would be uncovered by Russian researchers."
msgstr ""
msgstr "The author's work on this book in this direction established that the ancestors of the Tsvets had long lived in the Chernigov region — one of the regions of Little Russia, as Ukraine was then called. For a long time, there has been a version among the descendants of this family that in pre-Petrine times they bore the surname Kolyadinsky, with which their rights to nobility were inextricably linked. However, after the Mazepa rebellion (1908—1709), in which one of M.S. Tsvet's ancestors was involved, his relatives renounced the surname Kolyadinsky and thereby their noble title, taking as their new surname their nickname Tsvet-Samotsvet, and then — simply Tsvet[^28.1]. However, unofficially, the Tsvets were still called Tsvet-Samotsvets for a long time, as evidenced by some items with the initials \"Ts-S\" preserved since ancient times by their descendants."
msgstr "It is difficult to judge now to what extent this version corresponds to reality. It is confusing that the surname Kolyadinsky is not in any genealogical inventories. It is not even indirectly mentioned in the detailed 4-volume \"Little Russian Genealogical Book\" (Kyiv, 1908—1914) by V.L. Modzalevsky. Meanwhile, the genealogical books list such noble families as the Galagans, Miroviches, and others, who were directly involved in the Mazepa conspiracy but did not change their family name. Probably, the nobility of certain Kolyadinskys was obtained not by virtue of the antiquity of the family, but for conscientious state service in the 18th century, and the true reasons for renouncing it remained unknown to descendants and are not recorded anywhere."
msgstr "As for the surnames Samotsvet and Tsvet, they are found in a number of documents, indeed, after 1709, i.e., after the Mazepa rebellion. The earliest mention from October 1734, which was found in the Rumyantsev inventory of Little Russia from the time of Catherine II, refers to Sila Samotsvet — a courtyard clerk of M.I. Gudovicheva. The same S. Samotsvet is mentioned in the same inventory for 1738 and posthumously for 1768 in the position of a clerk of one of the Cossack hundreds \\[2\\]. Later, the Samotsvets are repeatedly listed in the Inventory of the Novgorod-Seversky Viceroyalty for 1775—1781 \\[3\\], but subsequently, this surname is almost untraceable, giving way to the surname Tsvet."
msgstr "Unfortunately, the First World War prevented V.L. Modzalevsky from publishing the final 5th volume of his \"Little Russian Genealogical Book,\" which included surnames from the letter \"T\" to the letter \"Ya,\" and thus information about the Tsvets. In the manuscript department of the Central Scientific Library of the Ukrainian Academy of Sciences, the working card index of this publication by Modzalevsky has been preserved, partially including the material of the unpublished 5th volume. It contains a reference to the earliest mention of a certain Maksim Tsvet from Kyiv, who in 1760 \"enrolled\" in Königsberg University."
msgstr "According to the historian V.L. Modzalevsky, the Little Russian nobility of the late 17th — early 18th centuries \"were far from alien to historical, theological, and literary interests, but, of course, they did not stand in the foreground for them\" \\[4\\]. This characteristic fully applies to the ancestors of M.S. Tsvet."
msgstr "One of the first mentions of representatives of the Tsvet family is found in S. Smirnov's book \"History of the Trinity Lavra Seminary\" (1867)[^29.1], which has now become a great rarity. \"In 1763, from the Novgorod Metropolitan Dimitry Sechenov,\" Smirnov wrote, \"hierodeacon Nikolai Tsvet was sent to the Lavra, who declared... that he could speak German and French and translate books without any difficulty\" \\[5\\]. Becoming a teacher of French and German at the seminary, N. Tsvet rose to the rank of archimandrite, and in 1771 was sent on a mission to Beijing. Upon returning to Russia in 1782, he received a pension and spent the rest of his life in the Kiev-Pechersk Lavra, where he is buried \\[5, pp. 501—502\\]. It is quite possible that the named Nikolai Tsvet was the brother of M.S. Tsvet's great-grandfather — Mikhail Tsvet. Information has been preserved about the Chernigov judge Mikhail Tsvet (born 1784) \\[6\\], according to our assumptions, the brother of M.S. Tsvet's grandfather — N.M. Tsvet. Note that the names Mikhail and Nikolai were the most traditional in the genealogy of the Tsvet family, as evidenced by the genealogy itself, first presented in this book on page 30. These names were borne by the main character of our narrative and his older brother Nikolai, who died in infancy."
msgstr "M.S. Tsvet's grandfather — Nikolai Mikhailovich Tsvet (c. 1790—c. 1855) lived in Chernigov and was a very wealthy man. He died not a very old man when he was thrown by a horse while riding through the fields of his estate. N.M. Tsvet was married twice, and from both marriages, his lineage was continued by seven sons: from the 1st marriage — Pyotr (1814—1864), Yakov (1816—?) and Nikolai (1818—c. 1900), and from the 2nd marriage — Semyon (1829—1900), Miliy (1833—1898), Grigory (1834—?) and Ivan (1836—?). N.M. Tsvet bequeathed all his \"undivided capital\" — all movable and immovable property — to his two eldest sons, Pyotr and Yakov, thanks to which they both became merchants of the 1st guild \\[7\\]. The other children received some monetary inheritance. By the way, Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet (M.S. Tsvet's father) soon gave most of the money he received to his friend, who was facing ruin (Lyashchenko E.A., 1970, p. 3). Note, by the way, that this friend was Nikolai Aristov, whose wife A.I. Aristova later, having been widowed, would become the second wife of the also widowed S.N. Tsvet."
msgstr "Having become civil servants, Semyon and Ivan left Chernigov. The other brothers, remaining in their hometown, multiplied their fortune, and the Department of Heraldry of the Petersburg Senate awarded them certificates \"for hereditary honorary citizenship\" for large supplies to the state — in 1859 to the merchants of the first guild Pyotr and Yakov Tsvet with their families, and in 1860 to their brothers — merchants of the second guild Nikolai with his family, and the still unmarried Miliy and Grigory Tsvet \\[7, sheets 27, 62\\]. At the same time, the Senate reported that \"their brothers Semyon and Ivan, upon completing the course of sciences in higher educational institutions, entered the state service and by order of the government were excluded from the merchant class\" \\[7, sheet 3, etc.\\]."
msgstr "Of the three elder brothers of Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet, Pyotr Nikolaevich left the most numerous offspring, who still live in Chernigov today. Of the close descendants of P.N. Tsvet, only his grandson Alexander Petrovich Tsvet (1870—1908) lived and died in Petersburg, as reported by the monarchist newspaper \"Kievlyanin\" (1908, No. 124) — the organ of Russian landowners in Ukraine. Although the genealogical roots of the Tsvet family were Ukrainian, the Tsvets themselves considered themselves Russian, without distancing themselves from Ukrainian culture. The lineage of the second heir to the father's capital — Yakov Nikolaevich Tsvet — soon ended: his children left him no grandchildren, and left us no information about themselves. The third brother — Nikolai Nikolaevich Tsvet — turned out to be more prominent in the genealogy of this surname thanks to his publications on beekeeping. In 1856, N.N. Tsvet purchased 10 dessiatines (about 15 hectares) of land near the Belous River, 5 km from Chernigov, created the Nikolaevka farmstead there, and an apiary, which ten years later consisted of a thousand hives and hollow logs. For exemplary beekeeping, their owner was twice awarded a large silver medal at the agricultural exhibitions of 1865 and 1883 in Chernigov, with an announcement about it through the local press \\[8\\]. In 1884, for new hive designs, the Free Economic Society in Petersburg awarded him its highest award — a gold medal. At his own expense, N.N. Tsvet opened an exhibition in Chernigov in September 1886 of the results of his 30-year activity as a beekeeper, presenting 12 types of hives of his own design, including a glass one that allowed observing the life of bees. The great interest in this free exhibition and its exhibits was also noted by the press \\[9\\]."
msgstr "N.N. Tsvet described the named activity more thoroughly in his small booklet \"What is necessary for proper and profitable rational beekeeping\" (Kyiv, 1890, issues 1—2), which he dedicated to the famous chemist A.M. Butlerov (1828—1886) — an active member of the Free Economic Society, who was seriously interested in beekeeping, especially using frame hives. N.N. Tsvet prepared one of his summer frame hive designs to be sent to A.M. Butlerov, but the unexpected death of the latter prevented the realization of this intention. Thanks to his successes in beekeeping, N.N. Tsvet, according to his assertion, even became a member of the Paris National Academy as an entomologist closely associated with the work of the Central Society of Beekeepers in Paris, which founded the International Entomological Society in 1866."
msgstr "N.N. Tsvet also had a penchant for recording Ukrainian folk tales and songs. According to one of such records by N.N. Tsvet, it can be assumed that he was the first to begin reviving the disgraced surname Kolyadinsky from centuries of oblivion and signing with it \\[10\\]."
msgstr "The versatile interests and abilities of N.N. Tsvet are evidenced not only by the above but also by a number of details about his ability to conduct property, legal, and other affairs[^32.1]. Such versatility was characteristic of many representatives of the Tsvet family, who manifested their outstanding qualities both in practical life and in the intellectual sphere of activity[^32.2]. We will be able to see this especially below, getting acquainted with the circumstances of the life and activities of Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet — M.S. Tsvet's father. However, first, let's briefly get acquainted with the younger brothers of S.N. Tsvet — Miliy, Grigory, and Ivan, with whom Semyon Nikolaevich was closer not only by blood (they were all children from their father's second marriage) but also in life. This connection was especially close with the youngest brother Ivan, who more than once came to the aid of S.N. Tsvet's family."
msgstr "Ivan Nikolaevich Tsvet graduated in 1856 from the Richelieu Lyceum in Odessa, where Semyon Nikolaevich had studied ten years earlier. In the same year, I.N. Tsvet began working as a geography teacher at the 2nd Odessa Gymnasium \\[11\\] and, apparently, lived his whole life in this city. The latter probably largely determined his closer contacts with his brother S.N. Tsvet's family. Here in Odessa, at the end of May 1896, his nephew M.S. Tsvet, who did not yet speak Russian very well, would arrive from Geneva and find shelter with his uncle I.N. Tsvet. M.S. Tsvet would bring his entire family there from Yalta in the summer of 1901 after the death of S.N. Tsvet, and would come in 1915 in connection with passing a competition at Novorossiysk University. Ivan Nikolaevich always tried to help his nephew whenever possible, with whom he was brought together not only by family ties but also by a common interest in natural science."
msgstr "It should be noted that in his inclination towards natural science, I.N. Tsvet began to follow in the footsteps of the natural history teacher of the Chernigov Gymnasium, Semyon Nikolaevich Kolyadin. It is possible that S.N. Kolyadin was either an illegitimate son or a foster child of N.M. Tsvet (the father of Ivan, Semyon, and the other Tsvet brothers), and he was given a surname similar to their original, but then renounced surname Kolyadinsky. The activities of S.N. Kolyadin manifested the same multifaceted interests that were characteristic of many representatives of the Tsvet family. Along with natural history, he taught technology and commerce at the gymnasium in 1811—1813 \\[12\\], and also engaged in technological improvements and translations. He published the brochure \"A newly invented, improved and most profitable method of purifying oil...\" (St. Petersburg, 1822), translated from German the book \"Description of a newly invented machine serving for shearing cloth and other woolen fabrics\" (St. Petersburg, 1821), and translated from Latin \"Plant Physiology\" (St. Petersburg, 1826). The last book is particularly noteworthy, being the first publication in Russia with such a title, and, many years later, phytophysiology became not an amateur, but a professional field of activity for M.S. Tsvet, although in roundabout ways, but still somehow connected with the first publisher of a manual on plant physiology in Russian."
msgstr "Perhaps Kolyadin was the first of the Tsvet clan to leave Chernigov and settle in Petersburg. It was Petersburg that became the permanent residence of another brother of Semyon Nikolaevich — Miliy Nikolaevich, who arrived there unmarried and lived in hotels. At the end of January 1873, living in the hotel of the merchant Bugaratov on Nevsky Prospect in building No. 118, M.N. Tsvet found an abandoned infant at the door of his room and took him in to raise. On February 3, 1873, he baptized the boy, named him Dmitry, and then by Decree of the Governing Senate of February 8, 1882, adopted him and gave him a higher education \\[13, sheets 3—11, 12, 15, 17\\]."
msgstr "In 1901, Dmitry Milievich Tsvet would open a private laboratory for chemical-microscopic and sanitary-hygienic research, certainly not without the help of his acquired father M.N. Tsvet. At that time, these two Petersburgers would happen to see their young relative with the unusual title of Doctor of Geneva University, who had come to Petersburg to look for a job, which will be discussed below (see p. 93). The address calendar \"All St. Petersburg\" for 1897 (p. 1130) contains the last evidence of M.N. Tsvet's residence in this city, and therefore of his death in that year."
msgstr "Much less information has been preserved about the third uncle of M.S. Tsvet who left Chernigov — Grigory Nikolaevich Tsvet. In the manuscript department of the Russian State Library in Moscow, among the papers of a certain Volkov, 3 promissory notes for 326 rubles were preserved, borrowed by him from Grigory Tsvet on April 1, 1908, and returned to G. Tsvet on May 13, 1909. The promissory notes and receipts for their payment were drawn up in St. Petersburg, so the possibility of G.N. Tsvet — a man wealthy enough to lend money — moving to this city from Chernigov is not excluded."
msgstr "However, of all the seven aforementioned brothers, the most interesting to us is Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet — the father of Mikhail Semyonovich — about whom until recently one could judge only by the dedication given to him as a \"thinker and doer\" in M.S. Tsvet's master's thesis (1901) (see below p. 106). Now the numerous archival and literary sources discovered make it possible to present quite fully the official, public, and literary activities of S.N. Tsvet, who played a major role in the life of his eldest son Mikhail."
msgstr "Semyon Nikolaevich was born in Chernigov on February 1, 1829. According to documents, his parents were the merchant Nikolai Mikhailovich Tsvet and Anastasia Akimovna Tsvet, née Rashevskaya \\[14\\], who belonged to one of the old noble families of Chernigov."
msgstr "S.N. Tsvet received his primary education at the Odessa Richelieu Lyceum — one of the most prestigious educational institutions of its kind in the South of Russia. In terms of the level of education and even the uniform of the lyceum students, it was in many ways similar to an institute. Thus, students of the philosophical and legal departments of the lyceum had not gymnasium stars on their uniforms, but galloons, and besides, from 1833 they wore swords as a sign of student status \\[15, 16\\]. It was on the basis of this lyceum that Novorossiysk (now Odessa) University was created in 1865, with which the main character of our book — M.S. Tsvet — will repeatedly come into contact (see below pp. 73 and 165—171)."
msgstr "Having graduated in May 1845 from the philosophical department of the Odessa Richelieu Lyceum, S.N. Tsvet in August 1845 became a \"self-supporting\" student, i.e., at his own expense, and not at the state's expense, at Kharkov University in the category of mathematical sciences of the 2nd division of the philosophical faculty \\[17, sheet 3\\], but in June 1848, after completing the 2nd year and transferring to the 3rd year, he left it, gravitating more towards economic education. Founded anew in 1805, Kharkov University was a relatively young university compared to some others, for example, Dorpat (now Tartu) University, which, although it received its university status in 1802, continued the traditions of its predecessor, the Academia Gustaviana, which had been operating since 1632. It was at Dorpat University at the physics and mathematics faculty in the category of physical sciences that S.N. Tsvet continued his university studies in September 1848 after transferring from Kharkov University. Among the subjects he studied at the new place of study, there were fewer humanities and more natural science disciplines such as chemistry, physics, geology, mathematics, zoology, botany, etc. Much attention was also paid to economic subjects and active mastery of foreign languages, especially German. Against each subject in his consolidated examination certificate were the grades \"gut\" and the highest \"sehr gut\" (German \"good\" and \"very good\" — E.S.) \\[18, sheets 6—7\\]. Almost all paperwork and teaching at the University was conducted in German, so S.N. Tsvet's candidate work on financial and economic issues was also written in it, written on 15 pages in a small, almost unreadable handwriting with many mathematical formulas and calculations \\[18, sheets 10—18\\]. The author of the work signed as Semen Zwjet (in the university certificate it is written Zwet), and based on the conclusions of the reviewers, \"the reasoning presented by him was found to be fully adequate for its purpose\" \\[17, sheets 15, 16\\]."
msgstr "In September 1851, S.N. Tsvet graduated from Dorpat University with the degree and rights of a candidate of the physics and mathematics faculty (for more details on this status, see below on p. 80). The certificate of graduation from the university, issued on August 14, 1852, states that Semyon Nikolaevich \"showed good knowledge\" both during \"his examination in the physics and mathematics faculty\" and \"in oral translation from Russian to German, in written translation from English to Russian and in oral explanation in this language... and, at the same time, is recognized as capable of conducting written affairs in them\" \\[18\\]."
msgstr "From the autumn of 1851, S.N. Tsvet improved his knowledge by attending lectures at universities in Germany and Paris \\[19\\] at the law faculties, and \"for the soul\" also attended some lectures at the medical faculty (Lyashchenko E.A., 1970). Upon returning to Russia, he entered the service first in the office of the Governor-General of Novorossiysk and Bessarabia (1852—1854) with the rank of collegiate secretary, and then was transferred to the office of the Chernigov civil governor to manage treasury and passport affairs in his hometown. Semyon Nikolaevich's authority grew especially after successfully conducting a number of financial audits, as a result of which in June 1858 he was transferred to the department of the Ministry of the Imperial Court with assignment to the department of appanages and seconded to the Syzran appanage office to the position of assistant manager \\[20, sheets 200, 200 rev.\\]. In Syzran, he wrote his first economic work, published in 1860 in the Moscow journal \"Sovremennaya Letopis\" and titled exactly the same \"Economic Activity and Legislation\" \\[21\\] as the article by N.G. Chernyshevsky, against which this work was directed with all the sharpness of its polemics."
msgstr "In the late 50s, after the Crimean War (1854—1856), two significant forces of Russian society emerged and then clearly defined themselves — liberals and revolutionary democrats, between whom relations sharply escalated over the issues of abolishing serfdom and the path of further economic and political development of Russia. Due to censorship conditions, the socio-political struggle of these two forces manifested itself mainly in disputes around literary-critical, philosophical, and other topics. It was initiated by the famous Russian writer and literary critic N.G. Chernyshevsky (1828—1889), one of the leaders of the Petersburg journal \"Sovremennik,\" who then became the central figure of the polemic."
msgstr "In connection with the publication at the end of 1857 of the royal rescripts that opened the beginning of the peasant reform, Chernyshevsky used the legal opportunity to highlight the peasant question from revolutionary-democratic positions, while criticizing the entire system of serfdom and showing its economic insolvency. Thus, in \"Sovremennik\" for 1858—1859, a series of his polemical publications appeared, among which was the detailed article \"Economic Activity and Legislation\" (1859, No. 2, pp. 313—368). Under the guise of a dispute about communal agriculture, the author advocated for a revolutionary path of agrarian transformations against the reformist path. He subordinated the demand for the nationalization of landlord landownership to the question of the \"quality of state power,\" in other words — to the question of the preliminary overthrow of the autocracy and the creation of a democratic republic \\[22\\]."
msgstr "It was against this work of Chernyshevsky that many authors unanimously spoke out, including S.N. Tsvet, condemning encroachments on private landownership and the property of landlords. S.N. Tsvet disputed the reliability of the sources used by Chernyshevsky and disagreed with his criticism of the redemption projects proposed by supporters of liberal reforms. S.N. Tsvet spoke very disapprovingly against the allegory \"about straightening the stick\"[^37.1], as a revolutionary call for the forcible expropriation of private property in land and means of production."
msgstr "The appearance in print of S.N. Tsvet's first article was largely facilitated by Nikolai Nikolaevich Tyutchev (1815—1878), who subsequently influenced not only the official but also the personal life of his young friend. A cousin of the famous Russian poet F.I. Tyutchev, N.N. Tyutchev was a very interesting personality. In the 40s, he collaborated in the journal \"Otechestvennye Zapiski,\" was a close friend of V.G. Belinsky (1811—1848), after whose death he took care of his family and left memoirs \"My Acquaintance with Belinsky,\" published only in 1914. Being in close friendship with I.S. Turgenev (1818—1883), N.N. Tyutchev managed the writer's estate in 1851—1853, and then entered the state service in the Department of Appanages as a branch head. During one of his official trips to the provinces, he met S.N. Tsvet in Syzran in 1856, and in April 1860, with his assistance, S.N. Tsvet, as a conscientious and capable worker, was transferred to Petersburg as an official for special assignments at the same department of appanages. The chairman of the Department, M.N. Muravyov, who soon became Count Muravyov-Vilensky (1796—1866), who \"liked\" Tsvet, soon appointed him branch head \\[23\\]."
msgstr "Having moved to Petersburg, S.N. Tsvet began to devote all his free time to literary pursuits. With the assistance of N.N. Tyutchev, he took an active part in the work of the Society for the Relief of Needy Men of Letters and Scientists, widely known later under the name of the Literary Fund. In February 1861, he was elected a member of this Society, \\[24\\] which was still very young. The idea of its creation arose in the late 50s among writers associated with the journal \"Sovremennik.\" Among them were N.A. Nekrasov, I.S. Turgenev, A.N. Ostrovsky, A.K. Tolstoy, I.A. Goncharov, and others. At the first meeting of the Society on November 8, 1859, they all became founding members along with A.N. Maykov, M.E. Saltykov-Shchedrin, N.G. Chernyshevsky, and others. S.N. Tsvet became personally acquainted with many of them and even made friends, in particular, with P.V. Annenkov, K.D. Kavelin, A.P. Markova-Vinogradskaya (formerly Kern), and others. Later, fate would bring S.N. Tsvet together with Victor Hugo, A.I. Herzen, and his son A.A. Herzen. But I.S. Turgenev left a special mark on his life. S.N. Tsvet would dedicate the brochure \"On the History of Russian Nihilism\" (1880) to his novel \"Fathers and Sons,\" which shortly before the death of the remarkable Russian writer would end up in Paris and be preserved in his library[^38.1]. Obviously, in connection with this brochure, a correspondence arose between Turgenev and Tsvet[^38.2], which unfortunately has not survived to our days."
msgstr "Soon, however, S.N. Tsvet was forced to leave his friends and Petersburg. Due to the capriciousness of the head of the department M.N. Muravyov, he was forced in September 1861 to become the secretary to the commander of three corvettes setting off on a round-the-world voyage. The detachment was headed by Admiral A.A. Popov (1821—1898), one of the outstanding shipbuilders of the Russian steam navy, the creator of the widely known at the time round ironclads, the so-called \"popovkas.\" In his petition of August 5, 1861, for the enrollment of S.N. Tsvet in the position of secretary of the round-the-world naval expedition, Popov wrote: \"...his printed literary works, the reviews of his comrades and, finally, my personal acquaintance with his way of thinking, give me the right to think that the appointment of S.N. Tsvet to the detachment will bring very serious benefit in the moral influence that he should have on all the gentlemen officers, not excluding myself\" \\[19, sheets 5—6\\]."
msgstr "At the end of September 1861, the corvettes sailed towards the Pacific Ocean, and one can judge the events that happened to S.N. Tsvet's friends in Petersburg from the letter-diary that A.P. Kern[^39.1] began to write to him in the hope of sending it to the addressee at the first opportunity. \"Listen,\" she wrote to S.N. Tsvet in her first letter dated November 20, 1861, \"you are a lucky man, however: look how we all love you. It alone is worth a lot that Nikolai Nikolaevich (i.e., N.N. Tyutchev — E.S.) speaks of no one in the world with such tenderness\" \\[25\\]."
msgstr "Kern's friendly disposition towards M.S. Tsvet was undoubtedly facilitated by N.N. Tyutchev, as well as communication in the Literary Fund, although, as Yu.N. Verkhovsky noted, she \"could have known him (i.e., Tsvet. — E.S.) already from her Poltava[^39.2] relations\" \\[26\\]. Kern's Petersburg letters to S.N. Tsvet included reports about meetings with mutual acquaintances, about a parcel sent to S.N. Tsvet's mother, and other everyday news, but the central place in them was occupied by descriptions of those events that were connected with the unrest of the students of Petersburg University, which led to its closure. Anna Petrovna knew that S.N. Tsvet, irreconcilable to injustice, would be particularly affected by these events. \"There is no doubt,\" she wrote, \"that we all, accustomed to seeing and hearing you, miss you, but I am almost glad that you are not here now. It is hard for us, it is unbearable for us to look idly at all these tricky actions (i.e., the introduction of new university rules that caused student unrest. — E.S.), how would it be for you?\" \\[25\\]."
msgstr "In two editions of A.P. Kern's \"Memoirs\" (1908, 1929), this diary is included under the lengthy title \"A story about events in Petersburg after Tsvet's departure for communication to him or forwarding, if the opportunity presents itself.\" However, it turned out to be impossible to establish contact with the addressee, and friends waited for some news from him himself. On December 9, 1861, Kern wrote in her diary: \"We were told that Popov would return, and we thought: 'Will Tsvet really return too?'. Suddenly he (i.e., Popov. — E.S.) appeared the day before yesterday with a visit... and not a peep about Tsvet: no letter, no news, absolutely nothing. We felt very sad...\" \\[25, pp. 365—386\\]. The last entry was made on December 18, 1861: \"Yesterday, Sunday, we were at the Tyutchevs, heard, firstly, fresh news about you...\" \\[25, p. 386\\]."
msgstr "The letters remained unsent[^40.1], and soon the addressee himself unexpectedly appeared. According to the testimony of Markov-Vinogradsky, S.N. Tsvet was suspended from his duties for \"liberal\" speeches against corporal punishment used in the navy and \"freethinking in general,\" and therefore was discharged ashore in the nearest English port. The official order of the naval department dated February 26, 1862, stated his dismissal due to illness \\[27\\]."
msgstr "The named order allowed S.N. Tsvet to continue his civil service and on May 1, 1862, to become an official for special assignments of the Ministry of Finance, with which all his subsequent official activities were connected, justifying the review of him as \"a man combining classical education with common sense and a mild character\" \\[19, sheet 6\\]. Exceptional diligence, great knowledge in economic and natural sciences, outstanding abilities as a writer and publicist allowed S.N. Tsvet, despite the harshness of his judgments about state disorders, to occupy a fairly solid official position. These character traits of his father were largely inherited by M.S. Tsvet."
msgstr "Soon N.N. Tyutchev's house became related to S.N. Tsvet. Here he had previously often met Ekaterina Alekseevna Tyutcheva (1841—1865), the niece of the master of the house. When N.N. Tyutchev and his wife lost their only ten-year-old daughter Olga, they asked the family of his brother A.N. Tyutchev, who had five daughters, to give them the eldest daughter Katya to raise and loved her very much."
msgstr "In May 1862, Ekaterina Alekseevna became the wife of S.N. Tsvet[^41.1], and soon the young couple left for Moscow, where Semyon Nikolaevich was sent on a business trip from May 5, 1862, to audit the Moscow Treasury Chamber. Having successfully completed this work in March 1863, he received an appointment to a new position as manager of drinking-excise taxes of the Poltava province and in the autumn of 1863 left Petersburg with his wife \\[20, sheet 201 rev.; see also: 28, sheet 18 rev.\\]."
msgstr "The next two years lived by S.N. Tsvet in Poltava turned out to be very difficult. A staff of officials was placed at his disposal to supervise 360 distilleries in the province. Being intolerant of any kind of abuse, Tsvet was inflexible and incorruptible in the performance of his duties. Because of this, conflict relations began to develop between him and local entrepreneurs-distillers who evaded taxation. Thus, the owner of two distilleries, M. Mazaraki, had to pay a significant fine for failing to fulfill product deliveries to the state and illegally selling the shortfalls. This decision could only be canceled or mitigated by the manager of drinking-excise taxes, whose place was taken by S.N. Tsvet. However, the latter not only did not cancel this decision but also actively opposed Mazaraki's attempts to plant more numerous small-scale distilling in the province. In response to a letter from this entrepreneur, S.N. Tsvet published a brochure \"Mr. Mazaraki's Statement on Distilling in the Poltava Province\" (1864), where he outlined his initiatives to streamline and develop the distilling business in the Poltava region. At the same time, he published the brochure \"On the Preparation of Malt in Simple Huts\" \\[1864\\], which also described the method he proposed for determining with the help of iodine and a saccharometer the amount of sugar lost by distillers during distilling \\[Cited from: 29\\]."
msgstr "In turn, Mazaraki came out with a response brochure, true, with some delay — already after Tsvet's departure from Poltava. Obviously, this is what gave him special courage to protest against the \"two-year dominance\" in the Administration of the one whose subordinates he called \"pupils of the excise-pedagogical school under the direction of Mr. Tsvet\" \\[29, p. 16\\]. Semyon Nikolaevich's irreconcilability to all sorts of frauds and mercenary attempts to cover them up led him to a clash not only with some members of the Poltava Zemstvo Assembly \\[29, p. 17\\], but also with the Poltava governor himself \\[33, sheet 36\\]."
msgstr "One can judge the atmosphere in which S.N. Tsvet had to work from his letter written from Poltava on October 6, 1865, to the historian of Russian law and publicist K.D. Kavelin. In connection with Kavelin's article on land credit \\[30\\], Tsvet disagreed with the hopes he placed on the government and on the nobles in the newly created in 1864 local zemstvo institutions during the \"implementation of economic and financial restructuring in Russia.\" To confirm his opinion, he told Kavelin about the activities of the members of the provincial zemstvo assembly in Poltava observed by him: \"They began their labors with the fact that, without familiarizing themselves with the budget, nor with the means, nor with the upcoming activities, they rushed to the main goal of their adventures — assigning maintenance to the members of the Board... Having done nothing, not even having drawn up a sensible instruction for the Board, the assembly hastened to make elections of the members of the Board and dispersed 4 days before the expiration of the assembly term. So great was the consciousness in everyone that they were only talking empty words. But it was at the elections that the valor of the nobility fully expressed itself. Not a single non-noble was elected as a member (underlined by the author. — E.S.), and from the nobles they elected such fine fellows that upon the closure of the assembly their first act was: to run... I received a letter from Kursk from the chairman of the Kursk district board. He writes that in the Kursk province it is almost the same. And after this you expect from the nobility unanimously honest activity for the common good! No, Konstantin Dmitrievich, rather this generation of drones grown on peasant sweat and blood should disappear from the face of the earth. They are only capable of devouring what is ready, and acting like lackeys. Our children will see better things, will see new people, and we will go to the grave only with hopes, only having seen the plowed soil, in which the seed thrown by each of us will not be lost\" \\[31, sheets 2 rev., 3, 4\\]."
msgstr "At home, the troubles of the service retreated away. \"Happy 2 years congratulate happy 20 years\" \\[32, sheet 76 rev.\\] — read the telegram sent by Semyon Nikolaevich and Ekaterina Alekseevna on October 17, 1864, to Nikolai Nikolaevich and Alexandra Petrovna Tyutchev, who were celebrating the twentieth anniversary of their wedding."
msgstr "After the New Year of 1865, N.N. Tyutchev wrote in his diary: \"We just received a dispatch from Poltava that Katya Tsvet gave birth safely on January 8 at 5 o'clock in the evening to a healthy and large boy,\" and immediately another entry \"A telegram informs that Katya passed away on January 15 at 10 o'clock. We loved her like a second daughter, and she is gone, like our Olga. The blow was completely unexpected, since we, having received the first telegram, had completely calmed down\" \\[32, sheet 100 rev.\\]. And a month and a half later, the little son Kolya also died. \"Now poor Semyon Nikolaevich,\" wrote N.N. Tyutchev on March 2, 1865, \"is completely orphaned and will probably move here\" \\[32, sheet 115\\]."
msgstr "Semyon Nikolaevich took the loss of his wife and son very hard[^43.1]. Having no friends in Poltava, in these difficult days he was drawn even more strongly to those people close to him whom he had left in Petersburg. \"The day before yesterday,\" N.N. Tyutchev recorded in his diary, \"I received a letter from Tsvet, written between the death and the funeral of the child. The letter is the most desperate. He asks to be transferred somehow from Poltava, to crush him with affairs, but only to transfer him.\" And further, on November 10: \"He asked the count very much to summon him here, which was done yesterday by telegraph, and then to give him an occupation here or transfer him to another province\" \\[32, sheet 116\\]."
msgstr "As soon as permission to leave was received, S.N. Tsvet immediately left Poltava. We again learn about his life in Petersburg, entirely filled with work, from N.N. Tyutchev's diary. Here are some of the entries from July 19, 1865: \"... Semyon Nikolaevich is still here (i.e., in Krasnoye Selo, a dacha place near Petersburg. — E.S.), but more in the city, busy with work, and comes to us to rest\" \\[33, sheet 11 rev.\\]; August 1: \"Tsvet is still in Petersburg busy with work...\" \\[33, sheet 12 rev.\\] and others."
msgstr "In January 1866, Semyon Nikolaevich had another, but this time particularly acute clash with the Poltava governor, as a result of which \"Tsvet wanted to resign. They barely begged him to stay here (since there was an intention to send Tsvet from the Department of Appanages from Petersburg to another province. — E.S.) as an official for special assignments at the Ministry of Finance\" \\[33, sheet 36\\]. In February 1866, by a new order of the Ministry of Finance, he was sent to the Department of Non-Oklad Fees, and a month later \"by decree of the governing senate promoted for years of service to court councilors\" \\[20, file 80, sheet 202\\]."
msgstr "S.N. Tsvet took his new duties responsibly and began to carefully prepare for the upcoming trip to Tashkent \"for searches in the field of industry and trade\" \\[3, sheet 103\\]: he studied political and economic literature about the Central Asian outskirts of Russia, raised long-term, often secret correspondence concerning trade in these areas. Tsvet's three-month stay in Central Asia gave a lot of new information to the Ministry, and he met the New Year of 1967 again among his close friends at the Tyutchevs' \\[33, sheet 103\\]."
msgstr "The subsequent years did not bring any significant changes to the course of S.N. Tsvet's life. True, some acquaintances advised him to accept proposals to enter into a fictitious marriage with S.V. Korvin-Krukovskaya (later, by husband, S.V. Kovalevskaya), well known to him, who later became a famous mathematician, but he refused (Lyashchenko E.A., 1970, p. 2). All his time was taken up by trips around Russia and sometimes trips abroad. Of these, the most interesting was a business trip in May — June 1867 to Paris to organize a display of Russian industry and trade there at an exhibition. As a result of his active work in the field of developing the domestic economy, S.N. Tsvet was promoted at the end of 1869 to collegiate councilors, and in April 1870 was appointed manager of the Podolsk Treasury Chamber and awarded the Order of St. Anna, 2nd degree. In May 1870, he arrived at the place of his new service in Kamenets-Podolsk. Obviously, here, or perhaps even in Petersburg, his acquaintance with his future wife and mother of Mikhail Semyonovich, Maria de Dorozza, took place."
msgstr "Until recently, we knew even less about M.S. Tsvet's mother than about his father, i.e., only her surname and that she was Italian[^44.1]. From M.S. Tsvet's birth certificate, it follows that Maria Nikolaevna was born, approximately, in 1846 in the industrial Turkish city of Kütahya, the administrative center of the Kütahya vilayet, located in the western part of Turkey. About her parents it is only known that they were Italians and the father's name was Nicolo[^44.2]. Their place of residence in Turkey is explained, possibly, by those seven Venetian-Turkish wars that were fought in the 15th — 18th centuries and during which Venice lost almost all rights to Cyprus, Crete, and some other areas that finally went to Turkey along with the Italians living in them."
msgstr "The circumstances of Maria Nikolaevna's arrival in Russia are unknown. According to the report of E.A. Lyashchenko (1970), she was a pupil of the famous Russian poet A.M. Zhemchuzhnikov (1821 — 1908), one of the creators of the satirical literary image of Kozma Prutkov — a dull official-bureaucrat and rhymester, full of respect for himself and his \"genius.\" It was not possible to documentarily confirm Lyashchenko's report, however, it is known that from 1866 to the 70s Zhemchuzhnikov lived and worked in Germany, France, Switzerland, and Italy, and his correspondence with his wife, dating back to 1869, shows that in their family, besides daughters Olga and Anastasia and their governess Mademoiselle Maria, lived another Maria, whose name was always written not in Russian \\[34\\]. Zhemchuzhnikov came into contact with S.N. Tsvet on official business from 1862 \\[20, sheet 5 rev.\\], but besides that, they could have been acquainted by birthplace and lived in the Chernigov province, and also communicated in the Literary Fund."
msgstr "Nowhere has any information been preserved about the legal marriage of S.N. Tsvet with M. de Dorozza. In all documents since 1865, he was listed as a widower with no children. Apparently, their marriage was civil. Tsvet's parents did not live long in Kamenets-Podolsk. Due to deteriorating health, Semyon Nikolaevich submitted a petition on January 5, 1872, addressed to the tsar to grant him a long leave for treatment and to relieve him of the post of manager of the city treasury chamber of Kamenets-Podolsk. Having received permission for leave, S.N. Tsvet and his wife sailed in February 1872 to Italy. After an initial course of treatment in Genoa, which did not bring success, it was decided to go to the resort town of Arona by rail through such interesting museum cities as Alessandria, Asti, Turin, and Novara."
msgstr "Thus Tsvet's parents ended up in Asti, where their son Mikhail was born. The sickliness of the baby and the weak state of the mother's health increasingly worsened and threatened their lives. Soon the mother died, and all the care for the newborn fell on the father. Semyon Nikolaevich managed to find a wet nurse. Having entrusted his son to her care, he continued his treatment in Switzerland. During the trip, Semyon Nikolaevich's petition to be relieved of his post as manager in Kamenets-Podolsk was granted, and on May 17, 1872, he was transferred to Petersburg to serve in the Ministry of Finance. In a letter dated July 8, 1872, he wrote from Geneva to his new place of service: \"My health has improved so much that I suppose at the beginning of our July to return to Russia and be able to work\" \\[20, sheet 81\\]. Soon S.N. Tsvet left Switzerland, leaving his son in Lausanne in the care of the wet nurse[^45.1], but subsequently, to see him, he traveled there almost annually during his vacation, indicating as the reason most often the need to treat his illnesses[^46.1] or the illnesses of relatives."
msgstr "Thus, to see his son a year later on his first memorable birthday, S.N. Tsvet applied on May 7, 1873, with a petition to give him leave and permission to travel to Switzerland. The reason for the trip was named the need to accompany his sister[^46.2], \"suffering from a dangerous lung disease, to arrange for her treatment there\" \\[20, sheet 92\\]. On May 8, 1873, permission was given, and the trip took place. Similar trips to Switzerland were also made in 1874—1881 based on the poor state of Semyon Nikolaevich's health and the need for treatment at mineral waters \\[20, sheets 108—110, 114, 118, 122, 174\\]."
msgstr "Upon S.N. Tsvet's return from his first trip to his son in May 1873, an appointment awaited him as manager of the Grodno Treasury Chamber from June 27, 1873 \\[20, sheets 95—100\\]. In Grodno, he happened to see Adelaida Ivanovna — the former wife of Nikolai Yakovlevich Aristov, well known to him, who had a passion for card games. A case was told when, wanting to participate in a card game but having no money, Aristov asked Semyon Nikolaevich to vouch for him. He lost the game, but could not pay the solid loss. Fulfilling the guarantee, S.N. Tsvet was forced to pay Aristov's debt at the expense of his share of the received monetary inheritance. However, this incident did not become a lesson for the latter. Although Aristov's parents were very rich people, the son gambled away all the fortune received from them. Thus, the marriage to Adelaida Ivanovna Aristova did not give S.N. Tsvet any dowry, but placed on him in the future an intensified care for her shattered health (Lyashchenko E.A., 1970, p. 3)."
msgstr "In 1877, their daughter Natalia was born, whom N.Ya. Aristov's childless sister intended to make the heiress of her property (from it later the aforementioned debt of N. Aristov was returned to S.N. Tsvet). To consolidate the rights of the heiress in the future, Natalia was given the surname Aristova and even his patronymic (ibid., p. 3) and in no documents was she listed among the children of S.N. Tsvet, even in his last certificate dated February 5, 1900, where M.S. Tsvet was entered with the rights of a son \\[28, sheet 20\\]. But the son Alexander, born in 1881, not only received the surname Tsvet, but also became the first to begin writing himself Tsvet-Kolyadinsky (see below p. 142)."
msgstr "Family cares from the early 70s weakened S.N. Tsvet's connection with the Literary Fund, but did not detach him from literary activity. Over many years of work directly in the Ministry of Finance itself, as well as managing the Kamenets-Podolsk (1870—1872), Grodno (1873—1875), Estland (1877—1879) and Bessarabian (1880—1882) treasury chambers, he accumulated extensive experience and knowledge on the economy and finances of different regions of Russia and the state as a whole. On this basis, S.N. Tsvet wrote a number of small books, combining their publication into a general series, which he called \"On Issues of State Economy.\" Thus, in Revel (now Tallinn) in 1878—1879, his publications appeared: \"On Russian Currency,\" \"On the Drinking Question. On Improving the Current System of Excise Tax,\" \"On the Trade Tax,\" \"On the Final Organization of State Accounting...,\" \"On Customs Tariffs and Free Trade\" — and outside this series the book \"Landownership and Agriculture\" (Revel, 1878). S.N. Tsvet's publications also touched upon some events of the political and cultural life of Russia. His pen belonged to the works: \"Spiritism and Spiritists\" (St. Petersburg, 1876), \"Russia and England in the Eastern Question\" (St. Petersburg, 1877), \"New Reformers 'on Four Dimensions' in Religion and Science\" (Revel, 1878), \"On the History of Russian Nihilism. Fathers and Sons\" (St. Petersburg, 1880)."
msgstr "Judging by the correspondence and memoirs of contemporaries \\[35\\], Semyon Nikolaevich did not have an accommodating character. An example of this can be his sharp oral and written speeches against spiritism, which often caused an unfriendly attitude towards them and their author. Thus, S.N. Tsvet's brochure \"New Reformers 'on Four Dimensions' in Religion and Science\" (1878) was directed against the publication of the famous Russian chemist A.M. Butlerov \"The Fourth Dimension of Space and Mediumism\" (1878), describing this phenomenon. In turn, Butlerov \\[36\\] sharply spoke out against S.N. Tsvet's brochures \"New Reformers...\" and \"Spiritism and Spiritists\" as, indeed, against other authors who treated mediumism with great doubt."
msgstr "S.N. Tsvet's attraction to literary activity grew, and he became increasingly burdened by his service. Particularly burdensome for him were those official circumstances in which he had to face the difficult fate of ordinary people and have no opportunity to help them. With deep bitterness, S.N. Tsvet wrote to K.D. Kavelin about the humiliating position in which representatives of the peasantry and Cossacks were placed at the zemstvo assembly in Poltava due to their inexperience and ignorance of the organizational, managerial, and financial affairs of the zemstvo, and also about how \"liberal swindlers\" manipulated them during the elections of the zemstvo Board and the adoption of other decisions \\[31, sheets 2, 2 rev., 3\\]."
msgstr "However, if there was even the slightest opportunity to provide such help, S.N. Tsvet immediately used it. Thus, having learned about how in the city of Slonim of the Grodno province, by illegal means, entire societies of peasants were brought to trial on the most absurd claims from the lessor and the justice of the peace, he again turned to Kavelin with a letter dated October 15, 1879, where he asked \"to do a great good deed for the unfortunate peasants\" and with the help of the Minister of Justice to protect them from unlawful significant penalties \\[31, sheets 5, 5 rev.\\]. Judging by the next letter dated November 26, 1875, with gratitude to Kavelin for assistance in bringing the named justice of the peace to responsibility \\[31, sheet 7 rev.\\], the request was heard."
msgstr "But such interventions did not always end successfully. Sometimes, as a result of intercession, S.N. Tsvet developed serious conflict relations with influential persons, greatly complicating life and primarily official affairs. Partly this circumstance, and to a greater extent the craving for literary activity, determined his future life choice. In 1880, S.N. Tsvet was promoted to actual privy councilors, which in military rank corresponded to the rank of general. The new rank consolidated his official position and allowed him to count on occupying a higher position over time. However, having received the right to an increased pension, he resigned. In a petition addressed to Emperor Alexander III dated February 6, 1882, S.N. Tsvet wrote: \"The scientific research undertaken by me, promising, to my utmost understanding, to be not useless for the common good, requires a prolonged absence from my place of service \\[20, sheet 186\\]. In a letter addressed to the Minister of Finance N.Kh. Bunge, which was attached to the petition, he explained his intention to continue the study of the role of the monetary system in the state that he had begun earlier \\[20, sheet 184\\]."
msgstr "Having formalized his resignation and received a pension, S.N. Tsvet already in May 1882 arrived in Lausanne to see his son Mikhail. The attention paid by the Russian law enforcement agencies to the actual privy councilor is noteworthy. Already on May 21, 1882, the Ministry of Justice sent a request to the Ministry of Finance to clarify the purpose of S.N. Tsvet's stay abroad, but having received the answer that he was there not on official business, but in the position of a retired person, it calmed down \\[20, sheets 198, 199\\] — the version of an illegal business trip fell away."
msgstr "It is possible that one of the reasons for S.N. Tsvet's resignation was his wife's illness. They tried to overcome Adelaida Ivanovna's progressive tuberculosis with the help of village nature and careful care for the patient. They also did not want to communicate with some colleagues who condemned their civil marriage. The reason for which was the undissolved previous marriage of A.I. Aristova."
msgstr "Therefore, after his resignation, S.N. Tsvet settled with his family in the Tver province on the Shchedrino estate near the city of Kimry and devoted himself entirely to political-economic and literary works. In the same year, his book \"Money of the 20th Century\" (St. Petersburg, 1882) was published, and two years later — \"Progress and Poverty according to Henry George. How modern civilization can perish and what are the means of salvation; a political-economic study\" (St. Petersburg, 1884. 117 p.)."
msgstr "In these years, S.N. Tsvet's attention was attracted by the journalistic articles of L.N. Tolstoy, with which he began to appear in 1882 and which then poured into the book \"What Then Must We Do?\". These publications condemned the ugly social reality of the then Russia, touching upon economic, political, and even global state violence with the help of troops and money. When S.N. Tsvet learned that on September 28, 1883, Tolstoy publicly refused to be a juror at the upcoming session of the district court in the city of Krapivna, Tula province, and at the same time came out with a denunciation of judicial orders and those who administered them, he wrote enthusiastically on October 19, 1883, to Kavelin: \"And how did the holy and great word-deed of Count L.N. Tolstoy in Krapivna echo in you? For me, this is the trumpet sound of a new era\" \\[31, sheet 14\\]. S.N. Tsvet also fully shared the religious and philosophical views preached by Tolstoy at that time."
msgstr "All this could have prompted S.N. Tsvet himself to turn to the same problematic, as a result of which he wrote the brochure \"State and Religion.\" His youngest son Vladimir wrote about its existence in his autobiography (1934), but no other information about it, as well as about itself, has yet been established. According to V.S. Tsvet, in this publication his father \"promoted the ideas of separating the church from the state\" \\[37\\], which could not but cause a negative attitude towards it and its author from officials and primarily from the Chief Procurator of the Holy Synod K.P. Pobedonostsev, an extreme right-wing public figure. It was because of this brochure that such tense relations developed between Pobedonostsev and S.N. Tsvet that the latter hastened to leave Russia, in which after the death of his wife Adelaida Ivanovna in 1884 nothing held him anymore. In the spring of 1885, he, together with his daughter Natalia and son Alexander, left for Switzerland (Lyashchenko E.A., 1970, p. 2), in order to finally give a family to his eldest son Mikhail."
msgstr "The childhood and youth years of Mikhail Semyonovich Tsvet passed in Lausanne. The mild, warm climate, the healthy air of the Alpine foothills, and careful care allowed the boy's weak organism to grow stronger, and already at the age of nine he became a pupil of the famous Galliard College in Lausanne. Among the pupils of the college up to July 1885, the name of M. Tsvet was also listed [^50.1]. In the city archive of Lausanne, there is no evidence that any of the Tsvet relatives lived in this city in those years; until 1885, Tsvet lived in the Monbel villa with a certain Rochard Laniel, and S.N. Tsvet came here almost annually on vacation to his son, but this time their place of residence became Geneva, where M.S. Tsvet was to continue his education."
msgstr ""
msgstr "Life in Geneva had a number of advantages. The moderate mild climate, the abundance of sunny days, the wide expanse of Lake Geneva, softening with its humidity the ozone-saturated Alpine winds — all this created conditions favorable to the health of children and the treatment of Semyon Nikolaevich himself, and besides, it was the center of the scientific and cultural life of Switzerland. Geneva had a university with faculties of natural sciences, medicine, law, and theology, and attached to it — a public library with a valuable collection of scientific books and journals, which was of interest not only to the growing children but also to S.N. Tsvet himself. The scientific society, conservatory, opera house, museum of fine arts with an exhibition hall could satisfy the most demanding scientific and cultural requests."
msgstr "The detachment from Russia was not felt so strongly in Geneva either. Among the many foreigners, there were quite a few Russians. Some came to be treated or spend the winter in a mild climate, others stayed here to raise children or study at universities, third sought emigrant refuge in the Geneva republic. The ancient city of republican and Protestant traditions since the times of Calvin, the Reformation, and the Huguenots willingly provided shelter to temporarily defeated fighters for religious, political, or social freedom. The Russian colony of emigrant revolutionaries and students settled in a certain area of Geneva, in the Landolt cafe Russian anti-monarchists spoke with abstracts and reports, and the library-reading room received magazines and newspapers from Russia."
msgstr "Thus, in Geneva there were all conditions to keep abreast of the events of the political and cultural life of Russia. This is also evidenced by S.N. Tsvet's correspondence with the famous mathematician S.V. Kovalevskaya. In a letter dated October 4, 1886, he wrote to her: \"I will allow myself to hope that you, Sofya Vasilyevna, will not take for intrusiveness my friendly address to you, as to an old good acquaintance. Although you, as I learn with heartfelt joy from newspaper reports, continue to be exalted in your beloved science, but I, an old mathematician, value and love in you not only the mathematical magnitude, but your whole elegantly beautiful being, and I am glad of the presented mathematical occasion to exchange a couple of words with you.\" Having then expressed his considerations about the illegitimacy of conclusions regarding the properties of space based on algebraic formulas, Semyon Nikolaevich asked Sofya Vasilyevna: \"What is"
msgstr "Your opinion of this wisdom, oh, many-minded nymph Egeria[^52.1]?\", after which he asked her to write about her health, life, and plans for the future. About himself he reported briefly: \"I settled with my orphans in Geneva. If you go to warm up in the south from your cold Varangian North — do not forget to drop in on your cordially devoted\", followed by the signature \"S. Tsvet\" \\[38\\]. In the postscript, the author of the letter wondered if his book \"Progress and Poverty\" had been passed on to Kovalevskaya."
msgstr "The invitation remained unanswered. Fate again separated these two outstanding people, disposing otherwise. To look after the children and study with them, the governess Ella Wilhelmina Ebner was invited. E.W. Ebner (born 1856) was originally from Munich, but, having been left an orphan, lived with her aunt in Switzerland. Knowing absolutely no Russian, she communicated with Natalia and Alexander at first with the help of their older brother and father, then taught them German and French, and then began to study with them according to the home education program. In everyday cares, she tried to make the children not feel like orphans, and they became attached to their educator (in the family she was called Ella Albertovna); three years later she became the wife of S.N. Tsvet. Later, her son Vladimir would write about her in his autobiography: \"My mother, née Ella Albertovna Ebner, Swiss by nationality, worked as an educator before marriage, later — a writer, a volume of \\[her\\] poems was published\" \\[37\\]."
msgstr "This marriage of S.N. Tsvet was the longest — 12 years and until the end of his life[^52.2]. Even when Ella Albertovna had her own children — Nadezhda was born in Zurich, and Vera and Vladimir in Geneva — she did not change her former attachment to her pupils. She treated the eldest of them, Mikhail, with special attention and love, possibly as someone closer to her in spirit and language, and he reciprocated. All the children were friendly with each other and maintained this friendship for life."
msgstr "The ten years lived by S.N. Tsvet in Geneva in constant communication with his children were of great importance for the formation of Mikhail's character and worldview. The father took a special place in the young man's life; he became for him a model of steadfastness in defending his convictions, unselfishness in relation to his duties, and loyalty to civic duty. Conversations with his father and good relations, which began in 1886 with his eight-year-old sister Natasha and four-year-old brother Alexander, allowed Mikhail to overcome the language barrier quite quickly — first to understand, and then to speak Russian."
msgstr "Everyone in the family loved Mikhail Tsvet for his liveliness of mind, modesty, inquisitiveness, and cheerfulness. He reciprocated and retained a deep attachment to his stepmother, sisters, and brothers until the end of his days."
msgstr "In Geneva, Mikhail Tsvet continued his primary and then secondary education. By the way, admission to an educational institution required a certificate of baptism for those who belonged to the Orthodox faith. Semyon Nikolaevich was an unbeliever, the children in the family were raised without a religious cult, so Mikhail Semyonovich was not baptized. It was then that a record appeared about his belonging to the Protestant faith, although his father was listed as Orthodox, and his mother — as Catholic. Considering the need for such a document for admission to the gymnasium, all the children were subsequently baptized. One can judge the attitude towards this rite in the Tsvet family at least by how the christening of the youngest son Vladimir was organized in Geneva. A priest was invited home[^53.1], but at the last moment they discovered that there was not a single icon in the whole house to perform the rite. Then they hastily found a female portrait tarnished by time, hung it in the corner, and draped it so skillfully with a towel and flowers that the clergyman did not notice the substitution."
msgstr ""
msgstr "According to the information preserved in the documents of the Geneva College of Saint-Antoine, \"Mikhail Tsvet entered the senior class of the real department of this college in 1887 at the age of 14 years and 9 months. In the same record book, it was stated that he was Russian by nationality, Protestant by religion, and his father was a former state councilor (in Russia)\" (Cited from: Dhéré Ch., 1943, p. 25). The chosen class gave students a secondary education corresponding to the course of gymnasiums and real schools in Russia. In college, Mikhail became especially close friends with E. Claparède[^55.1] and P. Dutoit[^55.2], comradely relations with whom were maintained in subsequent years. Many years later, recalling his gymnasium years, Dutoit noted that his school friend \"Michel Tsvet had a very original and wonderful character during all the years of studying together with him at the Geneva gymnasium (1889—1891)\" (ibid., p. 26). An even closer friendship connected Tsvet with Claparède, and not only in the gymnasium, but also within the walls of the university. Later they also corresponded and met during Tsvet's subsequent few trips to Switzerland."
msgstr "Tsvet's favorite pastimes in his school and student years were hiking in the mountains. The boy loved nature very much and at first with his friends, and then with his sister and brother, made close excursions to the mountains. Later they climbed quite far into the Swiss Alps. Tsvet did not change this habit even in the first months of his stay in Russia, making hikes in the Crimean mountains and again traveling through the Alps on his short trips to Switzerland. He also loved music. Tsvet's musical education was greatly facilitated by Ella Albertovna, who always closely followed his studies and was proud of his successes. Perhaps he would have become a good musician if his love for nature and thirst for its knowledge had not prevailed."
msgstr "It was this love that determined the path that Mikhail Tsvet decided to take after receiving his certificate of maturity in June 1891, testifying to the completion of the gymnasium course. It was decided to become a student..."
