msgstr "Footnotes"
msgstr "In many publications about M.S. Tsvet not only abroad, but also in our country until recently (see Richter A.A. et al., 1946, p. 229; Kleshnin A.F. et al., 1967, p. 343; \\[Anonym\\], 1968, p. 1691; \"Vecherniy Leningrad\" of May 18, 1972), an incorrect date of his birth was reported — May 19, 1872 — the date of registration of his birth in the magistrate of Asti. The reason for this inaccuracy was a questionnaire compiled by Tsvet in 1915, in which exactly this date is indicated (see facsimile of the questionnaire on page 185). Tsvet gave the questionnaire to Briquet, the data of the questionnaire formed the basis of his 1940 publication, from where other authors borrowed them. Since in the countries of Western Europe in the last century there was already a new style, Tsvet's birthday is May 14, 1872, and not May 31, 1872, as noted by the Ukrainian Soviet Encyclopedia (vol. 15, 1964, p. 573) and the calendar of memorable dates of the journal \"Chemistry and Life\" (1972, No. 1, p. 56). And sometimes one can even find the assertion that \"Tsvet was born... in Russia\" (Szabadváry F., 1966; Szabadváry F., Robinson A., 1984, p. 242)."
msgstr "For more frequent cases of using nicknames as surnames in southwestern Rus, see: N.M. Tupikov. Dictionary of Old Russian personal proper names. St. Petersburg. 1903. P. 19—20. On the Slavic origin of the surname \"Tsvet\" see: M. Moroshkin. Slavic name book. St. Petersburg, 1867, P. 87, 203."
msgstr "A reference to this book, as containing information about Nikolai Tsvet, was found in the card index of B.L. Modzalevsky, stored in the Manuscript Department of the Institute of Russian Literature (Pushkin House) of the Russian Academy of Sciences. The author's surname was listed illegibly on the card, the title — very abbreviated. Searches for the book in secular libraries yielded no results. It was possible to find it, and then clarify the title and the author's surname, only after contacting the library of the Moscow Theological Academy in Zagorsk (now Sergiev Posad)."
msgstr "See: Archive of the Chernigov region, f. 127, op. 3, file No. 2751 \"On providing information about the estate of Nikolai Tsvet\" for 1859 with a mention of the ownership by the 2nd guild merchant N. Tsvet of a stone shop and a cellar. In the \"Lists of populated places according to the information of 1859\" (St. Petersburg, 1866) in the Chernigov district, 5 versts from Chernigov, Papernya (Nikolaevka) is listed — an owner's farmstead of one yard with 13 inhabitants (5 men and 8 women). A paper factory and a distillery are also listed there. In the manuscript department of the Central Scientific Library of the Ukrainian Academy of Sciences (Kyiv cipher: III, 68327—68331) see 5 letters of N.N. Tsvet on 10 sheets to the lawyer A.F. Kistyakovsky on legal issues."
msgstr "Evidence of this can be the opening in Chernigov of a bookstore, belonging most likely to Pyotr Petrovich Tsvet (1867—1927) — the grandson of P.N. Tsvet, the main heir and continuator of the merchant business founded by great-grandfather N.M. Tsvet and which ceased to exist by the end of the 20s of our century. Thus, back in 1925, the journal \"Bibliologichni visti\" (No. 1—2, p. 113) reported on the organization of the sale of Ukrainian books in Tsvet's store."
msgstr "This refers to the epigraph to Chernyshevsky's article \"Is it difficult to redeem land?\" (1859): \"When a stick is bent to one side, to straighten it, one must bend it to the other side.\" This is a free translation by Chernyshevsky of the words of the English economist T.R. Malthus (1766—1834), said in connection with the justifications of his law on population: \"Probably, finding the bow too bent to one side, I bent it too much to the other, wanting to straighten it\" (see N.G. Chernyshevsky. Complete Works. M., 1950. Vol. 5, pp. 500, 941.)."
msgstr "A copy of this brochure with the stamp of the Paris Turgenev Library is currently located in the Russian State Library (Moscow)."
msgstr "This fact is not noted in any of the editions of I.S. Turgenev's letters. It was reported by E.A. Lyashchenko from the words of her mother N.S. Lyashchenko (daughter of S.N. Tsvet), who personally saw I.S. Turgenev's letters to her father. These letters were kept in the family, approximately, until the end of the 90s. One of Semyon Nikolaevich's nephews, who lived in Petersburg (probably D.M. Tsvet), took them for retyping, but did not return them."
msgstr "The famous Pushkinist B.L. Modzalevsky wrote about this woman of those years: \"... Anna Petrovna Markova-Vinogradskaya (1800—1873) — the very Kern (by her first marriage) with whom Pushkin was in love in 1825 and to whom he dedicated his wonderful poem 'I remember a wonderful moment'. At the time described, Anna Petrovna, already an old woman of 61, lived in Petersburg, where her second husband Alexander Vasilyevich Markov-Vinogradsky (1820—1878), whom she married in 1842, served as a desk head in the Department of Appanages (i.e., in the same place as S.N. Tsvet. — E.S.); the spouses, despite the great inequality of years, lived extremely harmoniously and happily, passionately interested in both literary and social issues, moving in a small but cultured circle of their friends...\" \\[23, p. 49\\]."
msgstr "In this statement by Verkhovsky, an inaccuracy is allowed: S.N. Tsvet lived in Poltava later — in 1863—1865. It would be more correct to say about \"Chernigov relations,\" when the Markov-Vinogradsky spouses lived in the village of Sosnitsy of the Chernigov province (1844—1855) and A.V. Markov-Vinogradsky, holding a position by election of the nobility, could meet S.N. Tsvet in the Chernigov provincial chancellery and introduce him to his wife in 1854—1855. In Chernigov, this family could also get acquainted with S.N. Tsvet's mother living there."
msgstr "They were preserved by Kern's granddaughter A.A. Kulzhinskaya (by stage name Daragan) and were first published in the journal \"Minuvshie gody\" (1908, No. 10, pp. 50—69) with a preface by B.L. Modzalevsky (pp. 49—50)."
msgstr "This marriage of S.N. Tsvet was his second. In the certificate dated April 14, 1862, he indicated that he was 33 years old and that he was a widower and had no children \\[20, sheet 2\\]. The same was confirmed by the director of the Department in a certificate dated May 29, 1862, giving consent to his second marriage \\[20, sheets 8, 21\\]. There is no information about S.N. Tsvet's wife in his first marriage."
msgstr "The death of Ekaterina A. Tsvet (née Tyutcheva) on January 15, 1865, and her burial in the cemetery of the Poltava Exaltation of the Cross Monastery was noted by I.M. Kartavtsev in the 2nd volume of his \"Russian Necropolis.\" There, next to his mother, her newborn son Nikolai Tsvet (January 8 — February 24, 1865) was also buried. On their monument was carved the inscription \"You cannot live without me\" (OR PGPB, receipt 1955, No. 2, sheet 92)."
msgstr "Hesse and Weil (Hesse G., Weil H., 1954, p. 31), who undertook searches for information about M.S. Tsvet's mother in Italian and Swiss archives, bitterly stated that no data could be found in any of them. According to Italian experts, this surname is not Italian; it is also not found in any of the Swiss directories."
msgstr "To the request of the Czech biochemist I. Hais to the city of Kütahya about whether there were residents with the surname de Dorozza there in the 19th century, an answer was received. It reported that during the Greco-Turkish war, i.e., after the First World War, Greek troops burned all the archives, and therefore it is impossible to clarify this issue now (personal communication from I. Hais)."
msgstr "In the service record compiled on July 18, 1872, upon taking up a new position, S.N. Tsvet recorded that he was a widower after his second marriage (i.e., with E.A. Tyutcheva), and left the column about the presence of children blank \\[20, sheet 84\\]."
msgstr "In S.N. Tsvet's personal file there is a lot of information about his various ailments in different years of service. According to doctors who familiarized themselves with this information and the circumstances of S.N. Tsvet's life, his immune system was weakened due to the presence of an open compensatory form of tuberculosis, which did not manifest itself externally. This circumstance can also explain the death of all S.N. Tsvet's wives from this disease, which was the more fleeting the younger the organism was, not having had time to develop antibodies opposing it. Probably, this was also the cause of the death of his firstborn Nikolai. The same fate would have befallen the second son Mikhail, if he, already unhealthy from the first days of his life, had remained in contact with his sick mother, and not with a healthy wet nurse. This is an assumption, but it seems plausible."
msgstr "In reality, S.N. Tsvet asked not for his sister, but for the wife of his brother N.N. Tsvet, Maria Nikitichna, who was seriously ill with tuberculosis and died a year later, leaving three young daughters. S.N. Tsvet was very connected with his brother's family, on which, according to him, after the loss of his own family, his affections were concentrated \\[20, sheet 108\\]."
msgstr "See: Le College Galliard et son fondateur. Lausanne, 1901. According to Hesse and Weil (Hesse G., Weil H., 1954, S.31), Tsvet's name in official Swiss documents was written differently: \"De Zevett\", \"Zevett\", \"Zevet\", \"Zvet\", \"Zsvet\", \"Cvet\", \"Tsvett\" and \"Tswett\". This made it very difficult to search for information about him and his relatives during this period. Only upon graduation from the university did Tsvet choose the last spelling."
msgstr "Egeria — in Roman mythology, a goddess who helped women safely deliver from burden (allegorically — an adviser, a guide)."
msgstr "In total, S.N. Tsvet was married five times: for the first time after graduating from the University (the dates and name of the wife could not be established), the second time — in 1862 in Petersburg to E.A. Tyutcheva (1841—1865), the third marriage in 1871 to M. de Dorozza (1846—187?) was civil, as was the fourth marriage in 1876 to A.I. Aristova (? — 1884), the fifth time in 1888 in Geneva — to E.A. Ebner (1856—1920). In S.N. Tsvet's certificate, compiled in Petersburg on February 5, 1900, i.e., three months before his death, only those three marriages from which children remained are indicated, and of the wives only the last one is named by name: \"Married by a third marriage to the maiden Wilhelmina Ella Ebner; has children born: from the first marriage — son Mikhail — May 7, 1872, from the second marriage — son Alexander — April 18, 1882, and from the third marriage: son Vladimir — April 13, 1892, and daughters: Nadezhda — July 7, 1891, and Vera — January 30, 1891. The children are of the Orthodox faith\" \\[28, sheet 20\\]. Note that for the reasons mentioned above (see p. 47), the daughter Natalia is not listed among the children, and the birthdays of Mikhail Tsvet (see above p. 26) and Alexander Tsvet, according to whose data he was born on August 30, 1881, are inaccurately indicated \\[39\\]."
msgstr "After this rite, in the metric book of the Exaltation of the Cross Church of the Russian Mission in Switzerland for 1852 in part I under No. 4, the following entry was made: \"On April 13, 1892, Vladimir was born and baptized on September 15. His parents: Actual State Councilor Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet of the Orthodox faith and his lawful wife née Ebner, of the Lutheran faith. Godparents: Hereditary honorary citizen Miliy Nikolaevich Tsvet and as his substitute Mikhail Semyonovich Tsvet. The sacrament of baptism was performed by Archpriest Dmitry Opotsky with psalmist Pyotr Spasovkhodsky\". A copy of the record was given to the parents on September 29/October 11, 1892, in Geneva \\[40, sheet 16\\]."
msgstr "Édouard Jean Alfred Claparède (1873—1940) — a famous Swiss psychologist and physiologist, Doctor of Medicine (1897), head of the Physiology laboratory at Geneva University since 1904, professor of experimental physiology (1908). In 1912 he founded the J.J. Rousseau Institute (\"School of Pedagogical Sciences\"). Member of a number of foreign scientific organizations, including an honorary member of the Petersburg Pedagogical Society. Author of 15 monographs on issues of psychology, medicine, physiology, and general pathology."
msgstr "Paul Dutoit (1873—1944) — physical chemist. Graduated from Geneva University, Doctor of Natural Sciences (1893), ordinary professor of Lausanne University (1898) and of mineral chemistry (1918—1943). By the end of the 30s, author of 40 works on general and physical chemistry."
msgstr ""
msgstr "The certificate of maturity issued to Michel Tsvet by the Geneva college (gymnasium) clearly testified to the attitude of its owner to different areas of the knowledge he had acquired (see fig. on p. 54). The first disciplines that were put down in the certificate were subjects on mastering the French, German, English, and Latin languages. With a six-point grading system, the young man's knowledge was evaluated in the range of 4.88—5.5 points. The highest score fell on chemistry, followed by physics (5.5 points) and natural sciences (5 points). The lowest indicators were for cosmography (2.25 points), geography (3 points), and history (3.25 points). These indicators fully corresponded to the natural science direction that was chosen for further study at the university."
msgstr "In 1891, Tsvet became a student of the physics and mathematics faculty of Geneva University. The choice of this higher educational institution was largely determined by his father. Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet had a low opinion of how the education of youth was organized not only in Russia but also in other European countries. He valued much more highly the public schools of the free republican Switzerland, and of the higher educational institutions he primarily named Geneva University, founded in 1559. This university was considered one of the oldest higher educational institutions with rich traditions of natural science thought."
msgstr "The first four semesters of M.S. Tsvet's studies at the physics and mathematics faculty were memorable in that the general course in biology was conducted under the practical and theoretical guidance of professors C. Vogt[^57.1] and E. Yung[^57.2]. Indeed, the lectures of such a famous naturalist of those years as Carl Vogt, an anticlerical, an advanced scientist with a critical philosophical mindset, who possessed the richest titles in the field of zoology, left an unforgettable impression. Tsvet was among the last students who had the chance to listen to Vogt's course. Professor Yung, who enjoyed Tsvet's special respect, helped Vogt conduct practical classes. Tsvet also attended lectures by M. Schiff[^58.1] on animal and human physiology, T. Flournoy[^58.2] on psychology, and L. Duparc[^58.3] on geology and paleontology. Lectures and classes in chemistry with C. Graebe[^58.4] and P.A. Guye[^58.5], and in physics with C. Soret[^58.6], which were so useful to him in his further research work, also gave Tsvet a lot. To the last two — Guye and Soret — Tsvet will express special gratitude for their assistance with his dissertation (optional diploma) work, carried out at Geneva University[^58.7] and granting the academic degree of Doctor of Natural Sciences."
msgstr "However, the greatest influence on the choice of the direction of Tsvet's scientific activity, according to his admission, was exerted by the botanists R. Chodat, and later M. Thury. Both of these researchers were distinguished by the breadth of their interests and knowledge, and especially by their desire to master and create new research methods. A little more should be said about each of these two professors."
msgstr "Robert Hippolyte Chodat (1865—1934) — botanist, geologist, biochemist, and plant cytophysiologist, Doctor of Natural Sciences (1887), extraordinary (1889), and then ordinary (1891) professor of botany, medicine, and pharmacy at Geneva University began his pedagogical activity at the university by reading a course on plant morphology in 1888. Soon taking the chair of systematics and medical botany and simultaneously conducting extensive experimental work at the Geneva Botanical Institute, Chodat later became known as a specialist in all areas of botany. However, he gave special preference to the study of green algae, plant physiology, and biochemistry. At the same time, the question of research methodology and its improvement always stood at the center of attention of all Chodat's works, which manifested itself especially clearly in his work \"On the methods of pure culture of green algae\" (1900) \\[1\\]. He also cultivated such a priority of methodology in scientific research in his students."
msgstr "For his works on botany and plant biochemistry, Chodat was elected a corresponding member of five academies, including the Russian Academy of Sciences (1924) on the recommendation of academicians S.P. Kostychev and V.L. Komarov \\[2\\], as well as an honorary member of a number of universities and societies, in particular the Moscow Society of Naturalists (1909). In 1944, when the USSR Academy of Sciences marked the 10th anniversary of Chodat's death, Ivan Vladimirovich Palibin, a researcher at the Botanical Institute of the USSR Academy of Sciences, who had attended his lectures at Geneva University, shared his memories of Chodat. \"An excellent lecturer, equally skillfully mastering the word as well as the drawing for the educational board,\" Palibin wrote, \"then in a short time managed to make the botanical audience at Geneva University the most visited not only by students, but also by many nature lovers and foreign scientists. Within the walls of the Botanical Institute and the university auditorium, rows of studying youth from all countries of the world found satisfaction for their scientific requests; among them there were always many Slavs, including Russians\" \\[3\\]."
msgstr "Tsvet's admission to the university exactly coincided with the beginning of Chodat's study (1891) of living chloroplasts in various plants and his development of a special methodology. Tsvet called Chodat his teacher and, in particular, continued research into the nature (composition, structure, and functions) of plastids of green leaves. At the same time, he specially noted Chodat's merit in developing a special dye for plant cell membranes. This dye, called by Tsvet the \"Geneva reagent,\" according to his review, \"renders invaluable services both by its instructiveness and by the liveliness it brings to histological pictures\" (Tsvet M.S., 1902, No. 28, p. 523)."
msgstr "A characterization of Chodat as a teacher was given later by the famous botanist, researcher of the flora of the Caucasus N.I. Kuznetsov, who visited Geneva University in 1908 on the eve of the celebration of its 350th anniversary. \"The teaching of botany is set up by Prof. Chodat in Geneva in an exemplary manner...\" Kuznetsov wrote. \"With the kind permission of Mr. Chodat, I attended one of his lectures on general botany, which the talented professor read to a full audience; before the lecturer appeared in the auditorium, Russian speech could be heard, since... there are very many Russian male and female listeners in Geneva\" \\[4\\]. Chodat masterfully wielded colored chalks when drawing diagrams and other drawings with which he accompanied his lectures. The listeners tried not only to write down in detail what they heard from the professor, but also to sketch his illustrations. The course of lectures read by Chodat was then included in his textbook \"Fundamentals of Botany\" (Geneva, 1907)."
msgstr "In 1893, Tsvet passed the examination for the degree of Bachelor of Physical and Natural Sciences \\[5\\] and decided to devote himself to the study of the life processes of plants. Such a decision was not accidental. According to the authoritative testimony of K.A. Timiryazev, made in 1909, Geneva already from the beginning of the 19th century represented \"the main center of activity of modern botany and the cradle of its newly born branch — plant physiology\" \\[6\\]. This was also evidenced by the vivid speech of the famous Swiss botanist A. de Candolle \"History of Geneva Botany,\" delivered at the solemn meeting of the Geneva Academy on June 14, 1830 \\[7\\]."
msgstr "One can judge the exemplary state of the botanical institutions of Geneva at the end of the 19th century from the description by V.I. Lipsky \\[8\\]. The Geneva botanical school preserved and developed the traditions of its founders, including the naturalists Ch. Bonnet, J. Senebier, and T. de Saussure, known to the entire scientific world, who made a significant contribution to the discovery and study of the nature of plant photosynthesis. In memory of these scientists, back in the middle of the last century, their busts were installed in front of the main greenhouse of the botanical garden, which have survived to this day."
msgstr ""
msgstr "The works of Bonnet, Senebier, and Saussure on the life of plants already in the last century gained recognition among the still few plant physiologists of that time, who deservedly appreciated the scientific contribution of the Geneva naturalists. Thus, Timiryazev, recalling his trip to Geneva at the very beginning of his creative path in 1868, wrote: \"I was drawn to Geneva not only by the beauty of this wonderful city, as if floating between two azures against the backdrop of the distant Mont Blanc, not only by its glorious past, especially the memories of Rousseau, whom I had been accustomed to admire almost since childhood. As a botanist-physiologist, I strove to see my Mecca, i.e., the place where my science was born\" \\[9\\]. By the end of the 19th century, the study of plant life occupied a significant place in the university course. This is convincingly evidenced by the publications of the director of the Geneva Botanical Garden, privat-docent of the university J. Briquet \\[10, 11\\], one of the university teachers, and then colleagues and friends of Tsvet."
msgstr "Tsvet carried out his first research at the Institute of Botany under the guidance of R. Chodat. It was devoted to the study of the anatomical features of the internodal leptome, i.e., bast bundles in Solanaceae, in woody plants of hot countries — Loganiaceae, and in plants related to them of the small South American family Nolanaceae. This research by Tsvet was recommended by C. Vogt in 1894 for the H. Davy prize[^61.1], awarded annually to the best student works of the physics and mathematics faculty. The jury consisting of C. de Candolle, Chodat, and Thury unanimously decided to divide this prize between Tsvet and another of his classmates. Unfortunately, this work by Tsvet was not published in full. One of the fragments was printed in 1894 in the \"Bulletin de l'Herbier Boissier\" (Tswett M., 1894, No. 1), and five years later some of its provisions received coverage in the \"Reports of the German Botanical Society\" (Tswett M., 1899, No. 13)."
msgstr "Thus, in his first publication in 1894, Tsvet acted as a plant anatomist, describing cases of teratological stem structure. At the same time, he proposed his own method of preparing preparations — for the maceration of parenchymal tissues and especially for obtaining a contrasting staining of the sieve plates of the phloem, he recommended using potassium and silver permanganates."
msgstr "However, already in this period, some change in Tsvet's scientific interests was outlined. He began to give increasing preference to such emerging areas of biology as cytophysiology and plant biochemistry. This could have been facilitated by the increased attention to phytophysiological and biochemical research by Chodat himself, who was one of the first to begin studying the biochemistry of the process of formation and transformations of pigments in plants. Thus, together with the famous Russian biochemist A.N. Bakh[^62.1], Chodat became interested in the nature of the oxidative enzyme peroxidase and traced its effect on pigments. Their joint publications on this topic immediately received high praise \\[12\\]."
msgstr "At the beginning of 1895, events unfolded at Geneva University that also influenced the direction of Tsvet's botanical research. Having deep respect and great friendly feelings for Emile Yung, Tsvet could not reconcile himself with those who opposed the decision to appoint Yung, after Vogt's death, as an ordinary professor of zoology and anatomy and head of the zoological department. Because Chodat also stood in opposition to Yung, Tsvet left his teacher's institute, refused his guidance, and moved to the university laboratory of general botany, which influenced the further direction of the young scientist's research."
msgstr "In this act, as in a number of subsequent ones, Tsvet showed uncompromisingness, the ability to value his opinion and friendly relations, intolerance to injustice and calculation in any of their manifestations. To the credit of Chodat and Tsvet, it should be noted that the conflict that arose between them then did not subsequently affect their attitude towards each other's works. Thus, Tsvet continued to consider Chodat his teacher, cited his works, and shared many of his ideas, including about the structure of chloroplasts (see M.S. Tsvet, 1901, No. 23, p. 121). In turn, Chodat, although he did not mention Tsvet's name in his works, often talked about his method in lectures, especially in that part of the course where chlorophyll was discussed[^62.2]. According to Chodat's son, his father considered the creator of chromatography a true pioneer in science (ibid.)."
msgstr "Tsvet valued the experience he gained while working with Chodat, in particular, on the use of permanganates for the manufacture of microscopic preparations. This technique was developed by him in his second university publication, related to the improvement of microtechnique at a more complex cellular level (Tswett M., 1896, No. 2). The interest of specialists in this work is evidenced by the fact that it was printed by three different scientific journals."
msgstr "It is significant that, having encountered already in his first botanical researches such phenomena as the specificity of the actions of various dyes, the dissimilarity of natural and artificial pigments, the diversity of manifestations of sorption processes, Tsvet did not brush aside attempts to understand their essence, although there was no need for this when studying plant anatomy. On the contrary, the young botanist's attention to these phenomena began to grow as the physiological and biochemical aspects of his experiments intensified. This was also facilitated by the new leader, who himself did not confine himself to the narrow framework of specialization."
msgstr "The university laboratory of general botany was headed by Jean Marc Antoine Thury (1822—1905) — one of the oldest professors of Geneva University since 1856, an outstanding researcher and teacher from the generation of encyclopedists, author of numerous and diverse works in such areas as mechanics and technological mechanics, physics and astronomy, natural history (including botany), as well as philosophy and social economy. At the center of attention of the workers of this laboratory was the organography of plants (as plant anatomy and physiology were then called). Here, one of the most promising botanical disciplines — cytophysiology — was successfully formed and developed."
msgstr "It was this area of research that Tsvet chose as the topic of his doctoral dissertation. In its preparation, great help was provided by Thury not only as the head of the laboratory where it was carried out, but also as an ideologist determining the direction of priority efforts in the work of the entire team, which is the improvement of the methodology of the research being conducted. This also explains Thury's own inclination to design new instruments for studying plant life, as evidenced by many of his publications. Thury also developed such an inclination in his students and colleagues. Often the laboratory's instruments were made by its employees themselves in accordance with the specifics of the experiments they conducted."
msgstr "Tsvet was very impressed by the orientation towards the search for and construction of new methods and instruments. His second publication in the \"Bulletin\" of the laboratory was directly related to this, devoted to the description of the device and operation of his own model of an instrument for studying the transpiration (i.e., evaporation) of plants, which he called an idiometer (Tswett M., 1896, No. 3). In a note, the author explained the origin of the name of the instrument from the Greek words \"idio\" (îdios) — own, peculiar, special, — and \"...meter\" (metron) — measure. As we can see, such a name informs not so much about the functional purpose of the instrument as about its originality. By the way, in the named journal, Tsvet's publication was followed by Thury's report on the creation of his instrument, the antitypiometer (an indicator of plant resistance), in their botanical laboratory."
msgstr "The activities of the university laboratory of general botany in those years were well shown by J. Briquet in the aforementioned article \"The Laboratory of General Botany of Geneva University at the National Swiss Exhibition in Geneva in 1896\" \\[10\\]. Having received its offprint from the author, Tsvet was delighted. \"Thank you, dear sir, for sending your wonderful article,\" he wrote on the eve of the New Year 1897, \"...The article about the laboratory is a little masterpiece of its kind, and I laughed a lot reading some pages; Mr. Chodat, I think, also laughed, although for a different reason\" \\[13\\]."
msgstr "The research work on his doctoral dissertation closely befriended Tsvet with Briquet[^64.1], whom he had known well since he was a student, attending the practical classes in botany conducted by him. In the university laboratory, Tsvet worked side by side with another doctoral student, B.P.G. Hochreutiner[^64.2], on whose initiative almost half a century later the first article about Tsvet by Ch. Dhéré \"Mikhail Tsvet. The creator of chromatographic adsorption analysis. His life, his works on chlorophyllins\" (Dhéré Ch., 1943) appeared in the famous Swiss botanical journal \"Candollea\"."
msgstr "Tsvet's benevolence and responsiveness, as well as his liveliness, wit, and passion for science, aroused a feeling of sympathy for the \"charming Slav,\" as he was sometimes called, in everyone who communicated with him. Evidence of this is the right he received to \"eternal hospitality\" in Zofingia — a student society organized back in 1819 in one of the oldest Swiss cities, Zofingen. As Hochreutiner explained, the status of a foreigner did not allow Tsvet to become a member of this society, however, the general love and respect for him prompted his Swiss friends to make Tsvet in this way an equal participant in all the events held by the society."
msgstr "Passionate about his work and devoting all his time to it, Tsvet completed the experimental part of his dissertation in less than a year and already by the end of 1895 began writing it. He spent almost all this last stage of work at the villa of Claparède's family in Old Champel. Champel is a dacha suburb on a hill, immersed in the greenery of groves, parks, gardens, and vineyards. Some enterprising businessman, having bought a large field nearby, divided it into plots, built a two-apartment house with terraces on each, and began to rent them out at a moderate price. Among the tenants were Russian emigrants, who did not shun the native inhabitants. Young Genevans — Tsvet and Claparède — also willingly communicated with the Russians; the latter in the following year 1896 married Elena Afrikanovna Spir — the daughter of the Russian philosopher A.A. Spir, who told about her father in the introductory article of his posthumous edition[^65.1]."
msgstr "Tsvet particularly willingly accepted his friend's invitation to temporarily settle with him because by that time he was again left alone in Geneva, since his relatives, to whom he had managed to become so attached, had left for Italy. The departure was caused by the deterioration of the material situation of the Tsvet family, which had increased by the early 90s, and the need for S.N. Tsvet to re-enter the service."
msgstr "One can judge the changes in the life of S.N. Tsvet's family in Geneva from those letters that he sent to Russia, in particular to his former colleague at the Ministry of Finance N.Ya. Makarov[^65.2]. In the first years after arriving in Switzerland, S.N. Tsvet could entirely devote himself to literary pursuits. Then he came out with some publications, among which was the brochure \"The Worker and the Capitalist. An Attempt to Solve the Labor Question\" in French. In his letters of the second half of the 80s, he mainly reported various news of literary and political life, sometimes information about mutual acquaintances[^66.1]. At the end of the 80s, however, concern about the material shortage of the family increasingly showed through in them. \"With a great request in great need,\" S.N. Tsvet once asked Makarov to lend him money: \"As a family man, you will understand the need of a father of a family and justify my appeal to you\" \\[14\\]."
msgstr "In December 1890, S.N. Tsvet agreed on his assignment to the Russian Ministry of State Domains \"without maintenance with secondment on official business abroad\" \\[15\\]. However, the opportunity for such a business trip presented itself only in April 1892. S.N. Tsvet was sent to France \"to collect data on the matter of establishing special agencies in this country for the sale of our agricultural products\" \\[16\\]. His work was mainly connected with two French ports, Marseille and Le Havre, and consisted in establishing the possibilities of setting up trade in agricultural products through them. According to S.N. Tsvet's memorandum, \"increasing the export abroad of both raw and processed products of Russia can contribute not only to the development of national welfare and wealth, but also serve to balance our budget and restore our exchange\" \\[17\\]. At the same time, concrete proposals were given to revive trade with France not only in agricultural products but also in Baku oil."
msgstr "Having arrived for a short time in Geneva to see his family before his next trip to Le Havre and Paris, S.N. Tsvet in a letter to one of his Petersburg colleagues[^66.2] wrote with distress on July 21, 1892, about how insignificant Russia's trade with France was and how few Russian merchant ships there were in the port of Marseille. In the same letter, he reported with special warmth about his acquaintance with the publication in Switzerland of letters of Russian writers: \"These days I spent in the company of already disappeared good people of our generation — Kavelin, Turgenev, Herzen... The other day a small book of their correspondence came out here. A lot of interesting things for us, and for me in particular, who knew more or less closely the whole group to which they belonged\" \\[18\\]."
msgstr "After the completion of the business trip to France in 1892, another business trip followed on February 15, 1893. Not wanting to live separated from his family, S.N. Tsvet, despite his 65 years of age, decided to return to permanent service and on August 23, 1893, was appointed commercial agent of the Ministry of Finance of Russia in Genoa. Soon the large Tsvet family left for Italy and M.S. Tsvet temporarily parted with them."
msgstr "Occupied with one desire, to finish his dissertation as soon as possible and do it well, in order to start an independent life and begin helping his family, M.S. Tsvet devoted all his strength and time to this work. In those years, he maintained a closer acquaintance only with Claparède. Both friends were brought together not only by the years of studying together in college and at the university, but also by a passionate enthusiasm for science, the specialty chosen by each of them. At Claparède's villa, they intensively prepared for the upcoming final exams: Claparède — in medicine, Tsvet — in botany, zoology, and physics, which were successfully passed by them in 1896. Upon parting, which almost coincided with Tsvet's departure from Switzerland, he gave the hospitable host a photograph with the inscription: \"To my very dear friend Édouard Claparède in memory of four months happily spent in Champel. Champel, March 7, 1896,\" reproduced on the illustration insert (p. III)."
msgstr "These days of studying together strengthened the warm friendly relations between Tsvet and Claparède for many years, and at the same time gave rise to a commonality of their scientific interests. The latter manifested itself especially clearly in the discussion about the nature of plant tropisms and animal movements that unfolded between them in 1909. However, along with the discussion of any affairs and issues, words similar to these will be said more than once by both friends: \"I feel the greatest need to plunge into old university memories and see the only great friend that you are\" \\[19\\]."
msgstr "The result of Tsvet's first major scientific work — the manuscript of his doctoral dissertation \"Studies of cell physiology. Materials for the knowledge of the movement of protoplasm, plasma membranes and chloroplasts\" was handed over for review to Thury and other botanists. The dissertation consisted of two parts. In the first part, \"Plasma membranes and the movement of protoplasm,\" the dissertator tried to gain a deeper understanding of the physiology of the plant cell using the microscopic method. Based on various manifestations of the osmotic properties of the cell, he described new methods for isolating the protoplast. With the help of deplasmolysis, he managed to observe changes in the movement of protoplasm after a prolonged stay in some plasmolysing solutions. In the second part, \"Chloroplasts,\" Tsvet drew attention to the contradictoriness of the previously existing ideas about the structure of these cellular organelles and to the need to study the issue experimentally. He expressed his opinion on the structure of chromoplasts and on the state of chlorophyll not in an extracted form, but in the living chloroplast of the leaf. The dissertator summarized the ideas about the mechanism of decomposition of carbon dioxide by plants, discussed the definition of such concepts as adsorption and absorption or condensation, and also touched upon the question of the role of chlorophyll fluorescence in photosynthesis. In addition, he tried to find out whether chloroplasts can live outside the living cytoplasm, and depending on the answer, outline their phylogeny."
msgstr "The discussion of the dissertation was stormy. Despite the negative remarks of Chodat, who saw Tsvet repeating the previous works of G. Klebs, it was recommended for publication. Briquet spoke convincingly in defense of the dissertation. He particularly emphasized the originality of the research conducted by Tsvet and dismissed any suspicions of borrowing and repeating the results of Klebs's experiments. Briquet's opinion was supported by other participants in the discussion of the dissertation, and it was noted in the minutes of the meeting that \"this work, by its complexity and the method by which the author carried it out, stands above many other doctoral dissertations... It is an interesting contribution to the resolution of topical issues. We recommend that the faculty approve it; the dissertation was carried out in the laboratory of general botany of the university, directed by Professor Thury, under the supervision of assistant Briquet, who testifies to the perseverance shown by the candidate in his work over many months\" (Cited from Miège J., 1973, p. 19)."
msgstr "In the introduction to the dissertation, Tsvet expressed great gratitude for the help provided to his research by senior colleagues J. Briquet, C. de Candolle, C. Soret, P.A. Guye, and Al. Claparède. Professor M. Thury was specially noted. The first page of the dissertation was entirely devoted to the personal dedication of the applicant: \"To Professor Marc Thury with a sincere expression of deep appreciation and gratitude\" (Tswett M., 1896, No. 4, p. 125)."
msgstr "Probably, the debates that unfolded during the preliminary discussion of Tsvet's dissertation explain the conclusion about its publication placed on the entire next page: \"The Faculty of Sciences admits this dissertation to print, while refraining from evaluating it.\" The conclusion was signed by the dean of the faculty G. Oltramare on February 5, 1896 (ibid., p. 126)."
msgstr "However, before the time when this work will be published, Tsvet will still have to, although not for long, but very intensely perfect it, which we will see below from getting acquainted with his letters addressed to J. Briquet in Geneva from Russia. As a result, two versions of the named dissertation will be published, somewhat differing in structure. The first version of \"Studies of cell physiology,\" consisting of two parts of the above content, was printed in the \"Bulletin of the laboratory of general botany of Geneva University\" (Tswett M., 1896, No. 4). The second version was published in a more versatile and famous scientific journal — \"Archives of Physical and Natural Sciences\" in three issues corresponding to the three parts of the dissertation. The appearance of the third part in the new version is explained by the fact that the chapter \"Study of chloroplasts by the merotomic method,\" which was previously in the second part of the work, was here separated into an independent third section. Such a structural change testifies to the author's realization of the greater significance of the methodological side of his work. Indeed, then, at the dawn of biochemistry and cytophysiology, the improvement of methods for studying the plant cell was very essential, especially if it was connected with the study not only of the cell, but of its constituent parts, in this case, plastids."
msgstr ""
msgstr "The relevance of the problematic and the high professionalism in the approach to its solution by the novice researcher, noted by colleagues during the discussion of Tsvet's first essentially innovative work, were preserved in subsequent years. Confirmation of this can be the assessment given to the named research almost half a century later by the biochemist Ch. Dhéré: \"Even at present it is very instructive to read this beautiful work, amazing in the richness of its results, in intuition and originality of ideas. It is visible how already here that scientific insight of Tsvet began to manifest itself, which was one of the distinctive and remarkable features of his intellectual personality\" (Dhéré Ch., 1943, p. 26). Thus, in almost a year and a half, work was done that would have taken others a much longer time. At the same time, Tsvet prepared for publication and published the results of his research on plant anatomy, conducted at the Institute of Botany under the guidance of Chodat (see Tswett M., 1896, Nos. 2, 3). Of these, the first publication, later used in the dissertation, appeared in three different editions. Even with a cursory acquaintance with Tsvet's works of 1896, it is easy to make sure that it was with them that the path began, along which, after 7 years of intense work, their author will come to one of the most important discoveries of the early 20th century."
msgstr "The great physical and moral burden during the execution and discussion of the dissertation, the inability to spare himself in work, the inattention to his weak health could not but affect Tsvet's well-being, causing him severe overwork. He needed rest and therefore decided to undertake a trip to his relatives in Italy."
msgstr "There was no time to delay, since Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet back in 1895 expressed a desire to return to Russia with his whole family as soon as his request to be granted a position there was satisfied. Even from his Geneva letters to Makarov, it was clear how passionately Semyon Nikolaevich was interested in various events taking place in his fatherland and how he wanted to be there. Subsequently, he explained his decision to return to Russia by the fact that his \"children must have a homeland\" and, while he has the opportunity, he is obliged to help them in this. On December 1, 1895, S.N. Tsvet was appointed manager of the Taurida (or Simferopol) Treasury Chamber."
msgstr "However, due to postal misunderstandings, the notification of this did not arrive in Genoa on time, and the Tsvet family did not know about this decision for more than two months. Only after a repeated notification and an urgent request not to delay departure and to take up his duties did the Tsvets hastily leave Italy in early March. On March 16, 1895, they arrived in Simferopol."
msgstr "S.N. Tsvet never managed to see his eldest son before leaving for Russia. Of course, M.S. Tsvet, having received a higher education, was already an independent enough person to have his own opinion, but his father had no doubt that he would also go to Russia, and he was not mistaken. Tsvet initially intended to work after the publication of his dissertation in Switzerland or in Germany. As we will see further, in 1898 he will be given such an opportunity, but he will refuse the offer of his German colleagues. According to J. Miège, \"despite Tsvet's attachment to Geneva, despite the fact that he had acquired friends here, he was drawn to his homeland, with which he, however, was little acquainted\" (Miège J., p. 19)."
msgstr "Saying goodbye to his friends, Mikhail Semyonovich knew that he was leaving them and the places that had become dear to him, if not forever, then for a very long time. In the subsequent years of his difficult and restless life, he invariably remembered them with special warmth and always tried not to miss the opportunity, if such presented itself, to meet them again during his few trips to Switzerland."
msgstr "Right before leaving Geneva, Tsvet could not see Briquet, who had left for a botanical excursion to the Alps. This was all the more annoying because it was Briquet who was to carry out the publication of his dissertation, on the text of which Tsvet had some considerations. And Tsvet could not resist the temptation to work on it some more. Taking advantage of Briquet's absence, he took the manuscript from him in the hope of returning it at the end of his stay in Italy."
msgstr "Tsvet left Switzerland in the spring of 1896, somewhat delayed due to the discussion that unfolded over his dissertation. In Genoa, he no longer found his relatives. Tsvet decided to use his stay in Italy for rest and acquaintance with the organization of botanical institutions and research, however, he became so carried away by the inspection of scientific institutions that there was no time left either for rest or for editing the dissertation. \"My trip to Italy,\" he wrote shortly from Simferopol, recalling this trip, \"went splendidly. In Marseille I met Mr. Heckel, in Florence — Mr. Camel, in Rome — Mr. Pirotta and all these gentlemen from the Naples zoological station\" \\[20\\]. Tsvet was particularly delighted by the conditions for botanical work in Rome: \"Mr. Pirotta,\" he continued, \"took me every afternoon to his magnificent institute and to the adjacent garden, where there are the most beautiful specimens of tropical tree species. I was delighted, among other things, by Ephedra altissima, Grevillea robusta and casuarinas, growing here like trees, 5—10 meters high. How many beautiful works can be done having all species at hand in a living state! The institute is organized very well. The halls are very bright and spacious, there is everything: a chemical laboratory, collections in the galleries, a library receiving all journals. The herbarium is preserved in naphthalene. There is a fairly good collection of instruments: microscopes produced by various companies, a microspectroscope, an apparatus for monochromatic illumination, a clinostat of the Wortmann model (absolutely aphonous, etc.)\" \\[20\\]."
msgstr "Everything seen testified that the conditions for botanical research in Italy were no worse than in Geneva. However, this did not change Tsvet's decision to go to Russia even when he was offered the position of an assistant in one of the botanical institutions of Genoa. He refused it. The Geneva period of the scientist's scientific activity ended. Ahead was a life in Russia full of uncertainty."

msgstr "Carl Vogt (1817—1895) — Doctor of Medicine (1839), professor of zoology in Giessen (1847), professor of geology at the Geneva Academy (1853), professor of paleontology, zoology, and general anatomy at Geneva University (1872)."
msgstr "Emile Yung (1854—1918) — Doctor of Natural History (1872), professor of general zoology at Geneva University (1883), ordinary professor of zoology and anatomy there (1895)."
msgstr "Moritz Schiff (1823—1896) — Doctor of Medicine (1844), professor of histology and general pathology in Bern (1855—1862), professor of zoology and physiology in Florence (1863—1876) and professor of physiology at Geneva University since 1876."
msgstr "Theodore Flournoy (1854—1920) — Doctor of Medicine (1878), extraordinary professor of physiology and psychology at Geneva University since 1891."
msgstr "Louis Duparc (1866—1932) — Doctor of Physical Sciences (1887), extraordinary (1889) and ordinary (1892) professor of mineralogy and petrography, ordinary professor of geology and paleontology since 1895."
msgstr "Carl Graebe (1841—1927) — Doctor of Philosophy (1862) ordinary professor in Königsberg (1870—1878), professor of chemistry at Geneva University since 1878."
msgstr "Philippe Auguste Guye (1862—1922) — Doctor of Physics (1884) extraordinary (1892) and ordinary (1895) professor of theoretical and technological chemistry at Geneva University."
msgstr "Charles Soret (1854—1904) — Doctor of Mathematical and Physical Sciences in Paris (1876), professor of mineralogy at Geneva University (1879), professor of experimental physics (1887)."
msgstr "For more details about the named and other figures of natural science of Geneva University, see the directory: J.Ch. Poggendorff (ed.) Biografischliterarisches Handwörterbuch zur Geschichte der exacten Wissenschaften. Bd.1—6. Leipzig-Berlin. J.A. Barth. 1863—1938."
msgstr "The prize of the English chemist and physicist Humphry Davy (1778—1829) was established in connection with Davy's death in Geneva, where he came to meet his colleague physicist A. De la Rive (1801—1873)."
msgstr "Aleksey Nikolaevich Bakh (1857—1946) emigrated from Russia to Paris (1885) after the defeat of the anti-monarchist party \"Narodnaya Volya\", and in 1894 moved to Geneva, where he organized a home chemical laboratory. In 1915 he was elected president of the Swiss Society of Naturalists, Doctor of Natural Sciences of Lausanne University. In 1917 he returned to Russia, member of the Central Executive Committee of the USSR (1918), academician (1929), academician-secretary of the Department of Chemical Sciences of the USSR Academy of Sciences (1939), honorary member of the chemical societies of England (1943), USA (1943) and a number of domestic societies."
msgstr "This was testified by R. Chodat's student Charles Baehni (cited from: Dhéré Ch., 1943, p. 53)."
msgstr "John Briquet (1870—1931) — Doctor of Botany (1891), privat-docent of Geneva University (1892), director of the Geneva Botanical Garden (1898). Author of 276 publications mainly on plant systematics and geography. Developed and introduced international rules of plant nomenclature, as well as the correct names of a number of plants."
msgstr "Bénédict Pierre Georges Hochreutiner (1873—1959) — Briquet's successor, who headed the Geneva Botanical Garden and the journal \"Candollea\" in 1931, initiator of the collection of biographical information about M.S. Tsvet, partially provided by him for Ch. Dhéré's article (1943)."
msgstr "Claparède H. A. Spir. 1837—1890. Notice biographique // A. Spir. Nouvelles esquisses de philosophie critique. Alcan (ed.) Paris, 1899, p. I—XXII. This publication with the author's autograph on the occasion of gifting it to one of the members of A.A. Herzen's family is available in the Russian State Library (Moscow)."
msgstr "Nikolai Yakovlevich Makarov (1828—1892) — member of the Council of the Minister of Finance, according to the testimony of contemporaries, \"was distinguished by rare unselfishness, sincere love for work and modesty in relation to his activities\" (\"Golos minuvshego\" 1919, No. 1—4, p. 209). Makarov was also engaged in literary activity, was a member of the Literary Fund, his correspondence with I.S. Turgenev has been preserved (see \"First collection of letters of I.S. Turgenev\", St. Petersburg, 1884)."
msgstr "About one of his acquaintances — A.A. Herzen — S.N. Tsvet wrote: \"You were not mistaken... the author of the book on digestion is indeed the son of A.I. Herzen, formerly a professor in Florence, now — a prof\\[essor\\] of physiology in Lausanne. Only he is of a completely different sort than his father was. He is not at all interested in political questions; he is married to an Italian and has almost 11 children! His fortune, inherited from his father, \\[decreased\\] in some enterprise, and now he lives on his professorial salary\" (OR IRLI RAN, F. 170, Op. 1, d. 169)."
msgstr "S.N. Tsvet addressed his letter to Fyodor Romanovich, whose surname could not be established."
msgstr ""
msgstr "Odessa was the first city where, stepping off the steamer that arrived from Genoa, Tsvet first set foot on the land of his ancestors. However, for him it was not an arrival in a country unknown to him. He had read a lot about Russia and heard the stories of his father and older sister. At Geneva University, he often had to communicate with Russian students and doctoral candidates. That is why in his autobiography Tsvet will later write: \"In 1896 I returned to Russia\" (italics ours. — E.S.) \\[1\\]."
msgstr "Although in Switzerland he communicated little with his compatriots, and in the family only his father and older sister Natalia spoke Russian well, nevertheless Mikhail Semyonovich spoke Russian quite well, albeit with a French accent. This, however, did not cause him any difficulties in communicating with Russian interlocutors and when meeting the professor of botany of Novorossiysk (now Odessa) University Ludwig Albertovich Rishavi (1851—1915). From the early 80s, Rishavi stood at the head of the botanical school of this university. Upon arrival in Odessa, Tsvet immediately turned to him."
msgstr "This is how he then described this visit to his Geneva friends on July 6, 1896: \"In Odessa I was introduced to Prof\\[essor\\] Rishavi, a man of heroic build, somewhat resembling Victor Emmanuel (i.e., the then ruling King of Italy Victor Emmanuel III. — E.S.); he has a luxurious beard, he eats for four and drinks accordingly. Exceptionally affable. My letters of recommendation made a wonderful impression. I am encouraged that a position will be found for me in the near future\" \\[2\\]. At the same time, Tsvet also reported his impression of the phytophysiological laboratory equipment of the department: \"The laboratory is not so poor,\" he wrote. \"There are excellent gasometric apparatuses (improved by Timiryazev), one clinostat of the Pfeffer system, an excellent auxanometer. Mr. Rishavi told me funny anecdotes about Pfeffer, whom he dislikes, and Sachs he downright hates.\" Hoping for the possibility of obtaining a position at Novorossiysk University in the near future. Tsvet headed to Simferopol — his father's new place of service."
msgstr "The Tsvet family settled on the outskirts of the city in a house that was known to all Simferopol residents under the name of Steven's dacha. The first owner — Kh.Kh. Steven — was for many years the director of the famous Nikitsky Botanical Garden in Crimea. After his death, the owner of the house became his son A.Kh. Steven, with whom S.N. Tsvet was well acquainted. Being in permanent service in Petersburg in the position of Deputy Minister of Agriculture and State Domains, A.Kh. Steven leased this house and the adjacent plot of about ten dessiatines, abundantly planted with various trees and flowers, to S.N. Tsvet."
msgstr "Tsvet reported about his new place of residence to Geneva in the same letter dated July 6, 1896, as follows: \"Simferopol is a small provincial town where a bureaucratic spirit extremely prevails. Fortunately, we live out of town and are as if isolated from the world. Around everywhere are hills, in places stony and extremely steppe-like; if you cross a series of increasingly significant hills, you will come in a southeasterly direction to the foot of the mountains, the highest of which, Chatyr-Dag (about 1560 m), rises 15 km from here. I promise myself to arrange frequent excursions to these mountains.\""
msgstr "Although not as often as he initially supposed, Tsvet, indeed, repeatedly visited the Crimean mountains and Chatyr-Dag. From his hikes, he always brought plants that interested him, paying special attention to forms previously unknown to him and their adaptive features. Tsvet willingly shared the thoughts that arose as a result of these excursions with his Swiss colleagues. \"... I will send you some plants that seemed new to me,\" he informed Briquet in the same letter. \"If the plants of these places are of any interest to you, I am at your service to deliver them to you. Here on the plain the climate is very dry, in summer the sun burns, crazy winds... all this makes one assume in advance the presence of various xerothermal adaptations. In the mountains, on the contrary, it often rains and the peaks, all equally flat, are always green. The slopes are covered with rubble.\""
msgstr "Frequent excursions into nature and necessary rest were hindered by intense work on editing the text of the doctoral dissertation already accepted for publication in Geneva. Finally, Tsvet made the version that satisfied him. Reporting this to Briquet, he wrote on July 6, 1896: \"Dear sir! You will be very surprised when, upon returning from your journey, you do not find the manuscript of this unfortunate dissertation. Remembering how much labor it cost me and how much trouble it caused, I ask myself with great anxiety, what will happen to my future works?"
msgstr "I encourage myself, reasonably or not, only by the fact that I attribute my powerlessness to overwork, from which I have not yet recovered. Be that as it may, I did not manage to finish my editing during my journey. At the moment there are 40 pages with which I am completely satisfied.\""
msgstr "Finally, the first letter from Russia to Geneva was followed by a second letter dated July 18, 1896, addressed to Briquet: \"Dear sir! I have the pleasure to send you by the same courier the final manuscript of my notorious dissertation, it's about time! You can be sure that I have completely revised it and I hope that in its current form its value will not be in too striking contrast with the labor it cost me.\" Further, Tsvet shared his plans for the near future: \"Now that I have thrown off this work, which absorbed my best forces, I want to rest a little and prepare for the anxieties of the struggle for existence. With the help of Mr. Rishavi, in the near future I will start making inquiries and very much hope to find something by autumn. In the meantime, I will excursion through the steppe and the mountains and try to bring out of these excursions some trophy in the form of biological observations and interesting specimens. Together with my dissertation, I am sending you several specimens of plants of the surrounding flora. If they seem interesting to you, I will give myself the pleasure of collecting good specimens of endemic plants for you and drying them better. In a week or two I expect to start composing a work on osmotic pressure. However, I will try to frequently send messages to the 'Bulletin'.\""
msgstr "The subsequent three months did not change anything in Tsvet's life. Rishavi, who sincerely wanted to help him, unfortunately, could not do anything, since shortly before that he ceased to be the head of the botanical department of Novorossiysk University. This position was taken by the plant morphologist and systematist F.M. Kamensky (1851—1912). In the botanical laboratory, there was obviously a vacant position of conservator of the botanical cabinet, which from October 1897 was taken by A.G. Henkel. Perhaps one of the reasons for Kamensky's delay in giving a final answer to Tsvet's petition was the absence of a document about his scientific degree. For the same reason, Tsvet could not apply with sufficient grounds to any of the educational or scientific institutions in search of an appropriate position. \"Do not be angry with me, dear sir,\" Tsvet addressed Briquet again on October 9, 1896, \"that I am again bothering you about my dissertation and again asking you to speed up its publication to the extent possible. Having this edition in published form is a matter of paramount importance for me and does not brook the slightest delay. Without a dissertation and without a diploma, my hands are tied. I assume that at the moment the first half of the work is coming out of print. You informed me that the second half would be published without delay, and that I would be able to receive at the same time several more copies, besides those intended for the faculty. I need these copies very much in connection with my placement in a position.\""
msgstr "A week later, the long-awaited first copies of Tsvet's dissertation \"Studies of cell physiology,\" published in the \"Bulletin of the laboratory of general botany of Geneva University,\" arrived. \"Today it was a very pleasant surprise for me,\" Tsvet reported on October 15, 1896, to Geneva, \"to receive copies of my dissertation, the arrival of which was somewhat delayed because they had to spend some time at Odessa University. How can I sufficiently thank you, dear sir \\[Briquet\\], for all the care with which you surrounded the material realization of the publication! The illustration, as I find, is very successful, and the layout is almost flawless; all this makes me regret all those priceless moments that the proofreading must have taken from you. Thank you for providing a table of contents and modifying some polemical turns of phrase that escaped me in the haste of editing and the harshness of which I would certainly regret now.\""
msgstr "In the same letter, Tsvet reported that he intended to send the sent copies of the dissertation to Petersburg to I.P. Borodin[^76.1], whom he characterized as \"a scientist and a highly sympathetic person.\" \"In Odessa,\" he concluded, \"I seem to have received no result. I will impatiently wait for my diploma... The inaction to which I am condemned terribly weighs on me; I hope that in the near future I will be able to inform you of some decisive step forward...\""
msgstr "Soon Tsvet received a diploma certifying the conferment on him by Geneva University of the academic degree of Doctor of Natural Sciences. In response, Tsvet sent Briquet the promised detailed information: \"I will not go to Odessa,\" he wrote to Geneva on November 13, 1896. \"Mr. Borodin from S\\[t.\\] Petersburg, whose advice I asked when sending him my dissertation, notified me that from this moment he provides me with the right and opportunity to work in the phys\\[iological\\] lab\\[oratory\\] of the Academy of Sciences. This is already some result, and I think that in the conditions in which I will find myself, taking advantage of this offer, my further movement forward will be faster than anywhere else. So, I will set off in a week or two. The offprints of my dissertation have already been at the Odessa customs for 3 weeks. Imagine that until now I have not yet been able to receive them! However, this case is by no means exceptional. Such is the order that prevails in our administrative circles. I also did not receive No. 3 of the 'Bulletin', which you write that you sent to me."
msgstr "The article on osmotic pressure, which you remind me of in your last letter, is not forgotten. Only it has been 6 months now that I know absolutely nothing about what is happening in the scientific world, and therefore I am afraid that the literature on the subject has grown during this time. So I prefer to postpone the formalization of the mentioned article until the moment when I will be able to conduct the necessary bibliographic research. You undoubtedly tell yourself that I am an unreliable collaborator, since my manuscripts are never ready by the appointed time. I suppose that so far the delays can partly be justified by circumstances. However, I will make every effort to acquire some more Germanic punctuality in the future.\""
msgstr "At the end of November 1896, Tsvet left Simferopol for Moscow, and from there to Petersburg, where his personal acquaintance with Professor I.P. Borodin and Academician A.S. Famintsyn took place. With the latter, Tsvet developed such significant and benevolent relations for all subsequent years that he should be spoken of specially. Andrei Sergeevich Famintsyn (1835—1918) graduated from the natural department of the physics and mathematics faculty of St. Petersburg University (1857), prepared for the professorial title in Germany for two years (1858—1860), defended his master's thesis (1861) and was the first in Russia to begin in 1861 reading an independent course on plant anatomy and physiology at Petersburg University. According to the authoritative testimony of K.A. Timiryazev, \"Famintsyn was the first Russian botanist to choose physiology as his specialty, which was just beginning to attract the attention of German botanists\" \\[3\\]."
msgstr "Studies of the influence of the intensity and quality of light on photosynthesis, growth, and development of plants under artificial illumination formed the basis of Famintsyn's doctoral dissertation \"The action of light on algae and some other organisms close to them\" (1866), which then opened a series of his subsequent works on the study of photosynthesis and the photosynthetic apparatus of plants, namely chloroplasts and chlorophyll. At the same time, the research was conducted taking into account other processes of plant life, highlighted by him in the fundamental monograph \"Metabolism and energy transformation in plants\" (1883) and the first domestic \"Textbook of plant physiology\" (1887) (for more details about A.S. Famintsyn see: Kleshnin A.F. et al., 1967; Stroganov B.P., 1996)."
msgstr "What has been said allows us to understand the interest and participation that Famintsyn showed towards his young colleague from Geneva, who was studying a problematic close to him, and moreover from the same methodological positions, namely in the relationship of photosynthesis with other vital processes of the plant organism and in the development of his original instruments and techniques. Having become a full member of the St. Petersburg Academy of Sciences in 1889 and the first academician-phytophysiologist, Famintsyn achieved the creation in 1890 of the first academic laboratory for plant physiology or, as it was then called, the cabinet of plant anatomy and physiology of the Academy of Sciences. In the first years of its existence (1890—1898), the laboratory was located in a rented apartment on the 6th line of Vasilyevsky Island in house No. 23 and was officially intended only for the work of the academician and his laboratory assistant."
msgstr "However, creating the laboratory, Famintsyn strove to ensure that it could also be used by \"seconded\" botanists of Petersburg and other cities, i.e., all those wishing to conduct serious experimental work on plant physiology. Among the latter was Tsvet, who decided to continue the research of green cellular organelles — chloroplasts — begun by him in Geneva, and even settled next door to the laboratory on the 5th line of Vasilyevsky Island in house No. 38. In the late 19th — early 20th centuries, besides him, botanists studying various issues of plant physiology worked in Famintsyn's laboratory: F.F. Zelinsky studied relaxation phenomena in geotropic seedlings, V.A. Rotert — heliotropism, V.V. Polovtsov — plant respiration, A.I. Nabokikh — temporary anaerobiosis of plant seedlings, V.V. Lepeshkin — the release of aqueous solutions by plants. This botanical laboratory was the first special scientific research institution for plant physiology in Russia, which many years later was transformed into the K.A. Timiryazev Institute of Plant Physiology of the USSR Academy of Sciences (in 1990, the IPP of the Russian Academy of Sciences celebrated its 100th anniversary)."
msgstr "In the first years of its existence (when Tsvet began to conduct his research), the laboratory occupied a modest premises of only four small rooms: the director's office, a library, a chemical laboratory, and a dark room for experiments in the absence of light. The laboratory was equipped with a fume hood, a soldering table for glassblowing work, two sterilization apparatuses, and two thermostats. In addition, it had several microscopes, a microtome, a photometer, and equipment for microbiological work. The pride of the laboratory was the library, numbering about 5000 books and journals[^79.1]."
msgstr "The entire staff of the laboratory consisted of two people — director Famintsyn and a laboratory assistant. The position of the latter from the first days of the laboratory's existence was occupied by Dmitry Iosifovich Ivanovsky, now known to everyone as the discoverer of viruses, the founder of virology[^79.2] (see his portraits of 1896 and the early 20th century on the insert — p. VIII). Having defended his master's thesis in 1895 and received the chair of plant anatomy and physiology at Petersburg University, he left the laboratory assistant position in the botanical cabinet in September 1896. The vacant position was soon taken by the laboratory assistant of the university's botanical cabinet D.N. Nelyubov, whom Famintsyn characterized as \"a very serious and talented young scientist\" and with whom Tsvet, just like with Ivanovsky, developed very friendly relations[^79.3]."
msgstr "Nelyubov remained in this position until the end of his life. If Tsvet had arrived in Petersburg a little earlier, then perhaps he would have taken the vacant position in the laboratory of plant anatomy and physiology. \"It has been two weeks since I arrived here,\" Tsvet wrote to Briquet on December 30, 1896, \"looked around, settled down and am working in the academic laboratory of Prof\\[essor\\] Famintsyn, where I found a very good reception. I have again taken up the chapter of my work on Solanaceae to supplement and publish it. It is about the relationship of intra- and extraxylary leptomicous systems in the area of some small new observations. There is still no paid staff work, and running around giving private lessons is dreary!\""
msgstr "In the same letter, Tsvet reported: \"I met Borodin and Voronin[^80.1], and the other day I will go to Monteverde. It seemed certain that Monteverde would take Batalin's place[^80.2], however, his successor became Fischer von Waldheim (\"der Congressbotaniker\"). This case is explained by the fact that Monteverde[^80.3], beardless, despite being 40 years old, has too youthful a physiognomy!!! I am starting work on osmotic pressure.\""
msgstr "Neither in the Academy, nor in the university, nor in the Botanical Garden could Tsvet get any position. In the search for work, an unforeseen unpleasant circumstance was discovered: the status of Doctor of Natural Sciences acquired by Tsvet in Geneva had no force in Russia due to the difference in requirements for obtaining the scientific degree of master and, even more so, doctor of sciences. Tsvet's Geneva dissertation, in essence, corresponded to a candidate's work, performed then by students at will on given topics upon graduation from the university, and now its analogue can be considered a student's diploma work. Upon successful completion of such work, the graduating student received a diploma with the mark of candidate, which served as a kind of recommendation for subsequent scientific work, in particular, on a master's thesis. Without a master's degree, it was almost impossible to get a teaching position at a university. Therefore, it was urgently necessary to think about choosing a topic for writing a master's thesis and passing the master's exam. It is not surprising that he chose the study of chloroplasts and the chlorophyll contained in them as such a topic, i.e., issues already touched upon in the Geneva dissertation. However, the lack of a paid job did not give the opportunity to closely engage in the planned research."
msgstr "«I am very sorry,» Tsvet wrote to Briquet on February 22, 1897, «that circumstances prevent me from actively engaging in the promised article and science in general. I want to ask for the position of foreign correspondent in the local Botanical Garden, and I have chances for this. I am awaiting the arrival of the new director, Fischer von Waldheim, who, unfortunately, has just fallen ill. For the last three weeks I have been practicing English and German. The literature on osmotic pressure has increased significantly over the last year, and although at the moment I cannot yet master it, I have already managed to get up to speed. If I manage to get a job in the garden, then I will have the full opportunity in terms of time and tranquility to actively engage in scientific work.» And two weeks later in a letter dated February 10, he reported additionally: «The question of my candidacy for the position of correspondent will probably be decided these days. Be that as it may. Your recommendation to Mr. F\\[ischer von Waldheim\\], of course, can be very useful to me in the future.»"
msgstr "Tsvet's intensive studies of English and German at this time are explained by the vacancy of a foreign correspondent that opened in the Botanical Garden. An applicant for this position was obliged to have a good command of the main European languages in order to ensure the official correspondence of the Garden with foreign addressees, since all foreign correspondence passed through him. Tsvet was fluent in French, as his native language, and to a lesser extent used English and German, which is why he took them up specially. Of course, improvement in these languages helped him greatly in the future, when he began to publish in German journals and communicate with colleagues in Germany, but for now Tsvet's hopes were not destined to come true. He did not get the desired place, and therefore the opportunity to have a steady income."
msgstr "Russia met Tsvet ungraciously. The search for work for a number of months, the need to turn for help, as he wrote, to the \"parental wallet,\" the absence of close friends in Petersburg at first, as well as the cold and damp climate, which adversely affected his sickly organism, the new heavy burden of writing a master's thesis — all this caused Tsvet a depressed mood and regret about coming to Russia."
msgstr "He still shared his thoughts only with his Geneva friend. \"I have just received very sad news,\" Tsvet wrote to Briquet on June 19, 1897. \"Do you remember Madame Naumovich, this so sympathetic young woman who attended your laboratory classes, your lectures and your memorable excursions? She died in Paris almost suddenly, after a short illness, at the very height of her studies and on the eve of the realization of the goal for which she sacrificed everything. Thus depart the friends of the beautiful days of youth: some forget you, and of those few true and faithful ones that remain with us, death steals the best. You feel so lonely... I hope that when you have a minute of leisure. You will give me great pleasure by informing me about your affairs. Write to me how things are with you in the laboratory, in the botanical garden and in the university, and about your works and projects...\""
msgstr "This mood retreated only when Tsvet immersed himself headlong in research work. Here he forgot about all his troubles, entirely devoting himself to one goal — to get interesting data as soon as possible, which could be presented in the form of a dissertation testifying to his scientific competence. Gradually, connections with colleagues were also established, especially with young botanists — Famintsyn's students and those who willingly used the provided opportunity to experiment in the academician's laboratory. Of these, Tsvet became especially close to D.I. Ivanovsky and D.N. Nelyubov. In the St. Petersburg Library of the Russian Academy of Sciences, a number of Tsvet's works have been preserved with his dedicatory inscription \"To the highly respected Dmitry Nikolaevich Nelyubov from the author,\" including two of Tsvet's doctoral dissertations — the Geneva one of 1896 and the Russian one of 1910. The title page of the first of them with the author's autograph is reproduced on page 69."
msgstr "A major role in establishing Tsvet's close connection with Petersburg phytophysiologists was played by his joining the unofficial circle of the so-called \"Little Botanists\" as opposed to the association of \"Big Botanists,\" i.e., the Botanical Department of the Petersburg Society of Naturalists. According to the university charter of 1884, students were considered \"individual visitors to the university\" and, according to the testimony of contemporaries, \"had no right to any collective actions even in the scientific field\" \\[10\\]. Therefore, within the walls of higher educational institutions, the organization of any circles, including scientific ones, was prohibited."
msgstr "In order to have the opportunity to exchange opinions on news in scientific life, discuss the results of their first scientific research, acquaint their comrades with them and hear the advice of their mentors, students began to gather outside the university in private apartments. This is exactly how the physical seminary, the Small Chemical Society \\[11\\] and the circle of \"Little Botanists\"[^82.1] arose. Sometimes students took part simultaneously in two circles. Thus, Tsvet's colleague, plant physiologist A.A. Richter, was not only among the \"Little Botanists,\" but also repeatedly made presentations at chemical seminars \\[12\\]."
msgstr "Tsvet also maintained contact with the participants of the Small Chemical Society, in particular, with the initiator of the creation of this society in 1892, V.A. Yakovlev (1865—1924) and with its active figure B.N. Menshutkin (1874—1938), who left interesting memoirs about the first years of work of this society \\[13\\]. Later, in a report at the XI Congress of Russian Naturalists and Physicians, Tsvet will recall the opinion of the elder N.A. Menshutkin (1842—1907) that he had heard more than once — \"A chemist must learn to think chemically\" — and, developing it further in relation to himself and his colleagues, will convincedly state: \"A physiologist, however, must learn to think physically, chemically, and biologically, and to think independently\" (Tsvet M.S., 1902, No. 27, p. 57).
