This conviction was born in Tsvet during the years of communication with the \"Little Botanists\" and with friends of the Small Chemical Society, with whom he spent many hours at Petersburg University. Photographs captured him with his friends in the university chemical laboratory and at the entrance to it (see insert — p. VI)."
msgstr "The named student scientific societies played a considerable positive role in the formation of active figures of domestic natural science. Novice researchers received organizational skills in this work, preparation for further independent scientific activity in various institutions of the Academy of Sciences and higher education. At the meetings of the circle members, the criticality of their thinking and rhetorical abilities developed; many of them later became excellent lecturers. All this significantly affected the formation of Tsvet's abilities as a scientist, organizer, and teacher. According to Academician A.A. Baikov (1870—1946), the informal chemical seminary or the Small Chemical Society \"united young novice chemists and created that healthy scientific atmosphere in which emerging scientific forces developed and improved\" \\[14\\]."
msgstr "The circle of \"Little Botanists\" was created on the initiative of A.N. Krasnov and with the active assistance of A.N. Beketov in 1885 mainly from senior students of the university and young botanists. The name of the circle seemed to emphasize the qualitative difference of its composition from those \"big botanists\" who were members of the botanical department of the Petersburg Society of Naturalists, although some of them — A.N. Beketov, M.S. Voronin, A.S. Famintsyn and others — were invariably present at all meetings of the \"Little Botanists.\" Active participants in the circle were A.G. Henkel, D.I. Ivanovsky, B.L. Isachenko, A.N. Krasnov, N.I. Kuznetsov, B.I. Nelyubov, V.V. Polovtsov, R.E. Regel, A.A. Richter, G.I. Tanfilyev, V.A. Tranzschel and others. Botanists from other cities who conducted experimental research in Famintsyn's laboratory — V.V. Lepeshkin, A.I. Nabokikh, V.A. Rotert and others — also took part in its work. Tsvet also became a member of the circle."
msgstr "In January 1896, the association of novice researchers celebrated ten years of its existence. One of its members, V. Sidorov-Otradny, wrote an \"Ode to the decade of the society of Little Botanists\" and presented it to the participants of the anniversary meeting in the form of a beautifully designed text with a title page dated January 22, 1896. Here are some of its artless lines expressing sincere gratitude to fate for the established friendly communication of colleagues of different ranks, but equal relations:"
msgstr ""
msgstr "A special poetic dedication was made to A.N. Beketov as the founder of the circle, and even an anthem was written, where there were also such pathetic words:"
msgstr ""
msgstr "On the occasion of the anniversary, a memorable photograph was taken of the participants of one of the meetings of that time, presented on the insert (p. IV). Although Tsvet could not participate in this celebration, since he arrived in Petersburg at the end of 1896, he had the opportunity to feel its subsequent beneficial effect on himself. According to D.N. Nelyubov's data, from 1896 the activity of the community of \"Little Botanists\" began to grow sharply and became the highest in 1898—1900 over a quarter of a century of existence \\[17\\]. It was during this period that Tsvet happened to enter the unknown environment of Russian colleagues, test the results of his first scientific research there, and gain a benevolent attitude towards them, which could not but have a positive effect on the formation of the personality and the improvement of the professionalism of the young researcher."
msgstr "The \"Little Botanists\" gathered every week in the evenings at the apartment of one of the circle members, most often at Beketov's or Voronin's, as well as in Famintsyn's laboratory. Over a modest supper consisting of tea with sandwiches, in a relaxed atmosphere, without any strict meeting agenda, they discussed topical scientific issues, domestic and foreign botanical works, read abstracts, reports, and argued. We find a description of one of such meetings in Tsvet's letter to N.I. Kuznetsov — one of the active participants in the circle. \"On the 19th there was an annual meeting of the little botanists at Mikhail Stepanych's (i.e., at Voronin's. — E.S.),\" Tsvet wrote on January 31, 1898. \"It was, as last year, very good and quite fun. Nevertheless, it lacked un peu plus d'entrain (French — a little more liveliness. — E.S.), which was partly attributed to your absence. We honored the new or rather the future new academician Mikhail Stepanych. We drank to the health of those present and absent, to those who read abstracts, and to those who did not read, to those who listened to them, and those who did not listen, to those who did not miss a single meeting or did not attend, etc. So, a lot of toasts fell to your share\" (Cited from: Vaga A.Ya., 1958, p. 14)."
msgstr "However, such a joking mood appeared in Tsvet only almost a year later. In the spring of 1897, it was different. \"Nothing has changed,\" Tsvet wrote to Briquet on March 30, 1897. \"I am still waiting for a decision and dealing with administrative delays. I have finished the little anatomical work I told you about, and while waiting for the melting of the ice to put at my disposal the materials (aquatic plants) that I will need in my future dissertation, I am undertaking some physiological experiments with X-rays. For the mentioned dissertation, I intend to take up cultures in plasmolysing solutions again; I do not despair of achieving relative sterility, which will allow conducting long and undoubtedly decisive observations in terms of results. When the dissertation is ready, I will pass my 'master's' exam: this will probably be no earlier than in a year. Armed with a master's diploma, I will get the right to seek the position of assistant and teacher with the title of privat-docent...\" At the end of the letter followed a postscript: \"If you learn of any opportunity of this kind, such as presented itself in Genoa last year, of any vacant position, whether of an assistant or a teacher in a private or state institute, do not forget me, please, I ask you very earnestly about this.\""
msgstr "Tsvet's interest in the effect of X-rays on plants arose as a result of his acquaintance with one of the remarkable Petersburg scientists of that time — Pyotr Frantsevich Lesgaft (1837—1909) and with the work begun in the biological laboratory he had just founded. In the spring of 1896, Lesgaft suggested to those researchers who used the services of this laboratory to express their considerations regarding the setting up of experiments with x-rays \"in order to clarify their significance in the processes of life\" of animal and plant organisms, to give an answer to the question of how X-rays affect metabolism, development, and irritability of animals, I.R. Tarkhanov[^86.1] undertook, and in plants — V.V. Polovtsov[^86.2]. Since the laboratory did not have the equipment necessary for the experiments at that time, Tarkhanov conducted his observations in the physiological laboratory of the Academy of Sciences and in the same year published their results in the article \"Experiments on the action of Roentgen x-rays on the animal organism\" (1896) in the \"News of the Petersburg Biological Laboratory.\" At the beginning of 1897, such an installation was also acquired by this laboratory, so Lesgaft soon published the results of work using Roentgen rays. Due to Polovtsov's departure from the laboratory in April 1897, Tsvet began to conduct experiments with plants and X-rays."
msgstr "Acquaintance with Lesgaft and work with him were of great importance for the formation of Tsvet's scientific interests. Lesgaft was an exceptionally versatile naturalist, but his main scientific interests were related to medicine and biology. The author of a number of works on anatomy, among which \"Fundamentals of Theoretical Anatomy\" (1892) should be specially noted, he dealt a lot with issues of pedagogy, as evidenced by his widely known at the time work \"School Types\" (1884; 1890). He paid special attention to the teaching of natural science in secondary school and the basics of physical education."
msgstr "Lesgaft's scientific views diverged from the opinion of the majority of anatomists and histologists of that time. He was an opponent of the purely descriptive study of anatomy and a strictly morphological approach to the structure of cells of tissues and organs of any organism. Lesgaft considered such a direction obsolete and advocated a biological direction in the study of living nature, in which morphological formations are not only described, but also their structure is explained based on their functions. This idea was especially close and understandable to Tsvet. Being a botanist and initially engaged in plant anatomy, he also felt the need for a functional approach to the study of various plant structures."
msgstr "The technique that Lesgaft used in studying anatomy was later used by Tsvet in the anatomical work that he initially conducted in Famintsyn's laboratory. In order to \"causate\" certain morphological structures, Lesgaft often made a theoretical construction or modeling of the studied organ in accordance with its function. After that, he compared the resulting project with the structure of the studied organ, thereby confirming that it is exactly what it should be according to theoretical calculations. Therefore, Lesgaft called his anatomy course theoretical anatomy. A similar technique was used in Tsvet's article \"On the connection of external and internal leptomes of Solanaceae by means of intermediate leptome bundles,\" printed in a German journal in 1899, i.e., during the years of joint work with Lesgaft. In this seemingly purely anatomical work on the pathways of leptomes, or, as we now say, the bast of conducting bundles in plant stems, the author initially gave a functional characteristic of the phloem, through which organic substances synthesized by the plant move in a downward current to the tubers of Solanaceae. Based on the analysis of the function of leptomes, Tsvet came to the conclusion about the existence of anastomoses between them at the base of the stem of non-monocotyledonous plants in the case when the conducting bundles of these plants have external and internal phloems. It was precisely such anastomoses that he discovered in the medullary rays of Solanaceae. As a result, Tsvet came to the conclusion that \"the absence of connections between leptome bundles is a physiological absurdity\" (M. Tswett, 1899, No. 13, s. 233). The validity of this point of view was fully confirmed later by other researchers."
msgstr "Tsvet met Lesgaft in the most active period of his life — during the organization and establishment of the work of the Biological Laboratory he had just created and at the moment of the official opening of courses at it. Like everyone who knew Lesgaft, Tsvet was attracted in this man by his great purposefulness, scientific passion, and selfless devotion to the cause of enlightenment — traits so characteristic of Tsvet himself. \"The sole purpose of P.F. Lesgaft's life,\" testified one of his contemporaries, \"was science, which he served to the point of forgetting all other interests, being a rare example of unselfishness and lofty scientific idealism. P.F. Lesgaft's teaching activity aroused boundless respect for him from the youth, enthusiastic fascination with his ideas, and earned him all-Russian fame\" \\[19\\]. Thanks precisely to these properties, Lesgaft managed to achieve in the mid-90s in Petersburg the formation of an institution that provided exceptionally favorable conditions for scientific work for that time, which sometimes did not exist even in the academic botanical laboratory. This is evidenced, for example, by the presence of an installation for studying the effect of X-rays on plants, which Tsvet used."
msgstr "The circumstances of the emergence of this institution, in the organization of the work of which Tsvet also took part, are interesting. They are connected primarily with Lesgaft's teaching activity, which began at Kazan University in 1868, and from 1871 continued in Petersburg, first at the Military Medical Academy, and in 1884—1897 — at the natural faculty of Petersburg University. Being a talented and highly original lecturer, Lesgaft literally fascinated his listeners. The anatomy course he read at Petersburg University acquired special fame and soon became one of the most popular. Lesgaft's lectures were attended not only by natural scientists, but by many lawyers and philologists. His auditorium was always bursting with listeners and could not accommodate everyone who wanted to get to his lectures."
msgstr "In addition to lectures at the Military Medical Academy and the university, Lesgaft organized from 1872 at his home free lectures for everyone who wanted to study biology and medicine, including women, who at that time did not have the right of access to higher educational institutions. Soon these systematically conducted lectures began to be called Lesgaft's courses by students. One of his students, S.A. Ostrogorsky, — later the director of the P.F. Lesgaft Higher Courses — described these courses as follows: \"They were located in his small apartment, listeners and female listeners got there after registration, packed tightly in a cramped room and enthusiastically caught every word of the teacher... He read with enthusiasm, knowing neither fatigue, nor illness, nor holidays. In order not to waste time, he began reading lectures at 7 am. I remember how female listeners told that, meeting them running at 6 am, when in winter it is still completely dark, out of pity they were helped onto the service horse-tram platforms and given a lift to the nearest point\" \\[20\\]."
msgstr "One of the diligent attendees of these courses in the early 90s was a volunteer student of the university I.M. Sibiryakov. He was an unusually modest and even timid young man in whom it was difficult to suspect the owner of a multi-million fortune. Wanting to improve the miserable environment in which his beloved teacher lived and worked, and also to give him the opportunity to develop his ideas without detriment to his health, Sibiryakov transferred to him in August 1893 a significant capital (350 thousand rubles in gold currency). Lesgaft used this money only for public needs. He spent part of the funds on acquiring various biological preparations and exhibits, as well as on equipping a special room, and transferred the rest of the capital to the disposal of the Laboratory Council."
msgstr "Lesgaft put a lot of effort and energy into obtaining permission from the government to create a scientific institution at the expense of the funds received from Sibiryakov. \"The purpose of the biological laboratory,\" Lesgaft wrote in his petition, \"... is to enable persons scientifically prepared to engage in biological sciences, both for their personal improvement and for the production of special works on a given subject. The collections of the Laboratory are not intended for public viewing, but mainly should serve as material for scientific research\" \\[21\\]."
msgstr "Finally, after almost a year of petitioning, the Ministry of Internal Affairs allowed on August 12, 1894, to create such an institution under the name \"St. Petersburg Biological Laboratory\" with 4 departments: botany, zoology, comparative anatomy with embryology, and geology. Soon, in the \"Chronicle\" section of one of the capital's newspapers, a message appeared: \"For a long time in Petersburg, the absence of such an institution has been felt, in which persons who have devoted themselves to the study of natural sciences, but do not belong to the learned, professorial estate, could work. The laboratories and cabinets available at the university and the Academy of Sciences are inaccessible to private individuals, and they are designed for a small number of workers. Now ... this gap will be filled and Petersburg will soon be enriched with a new laboratory, intended specifically for the study of biological sciences\" \\[22\\]."
msgstr "A few months later, the Council of the laboratory was elected, which was to manage it headed by the director — Lesgaft, and the charter of the new institution was approved. The main clause of the charter read: \"The laboratory aims to promote the dissemination of information on natural science by providing persons who have received a higher medical or natural history education with the opportunity to practically study biological sciences free of charge under the guidance of experienced persons\" \\[23\\]. This opportunity brought Tsvet there in the spring of 1897."
msgstr "The botanical department was organized in the Biological Laboratory in August 1895 at the suggestion of the plant physiologist V.V. Polovtsov — its first head. In 1896, only two botanists conducted research in the laboratory. Besides Polovtsov, who was engaged in the study of bacteria developing on the seeds of some flowering plants, A.N. Kolobazin, a university graduate, set up experiments there. However, the newly created cabinet did not yet have proper conditions. According to the report on the work of the laboratory for 1896, to set up research on plant physiology, a more convenient and better-equipped room was needed. Since in April 1897 Polovtsov stopped botanical research in the laboratory and refused to head the botanical department, the care of creating an equipped cabinet for plant physiology and biochemistry fell to Tsvet, who was drawn to this matter on a voluntary basis. In 1897, with Tsvet's participation, a number of instruments were purchased for the cabinet in Potsdam, Paris, Leipzig, and other cities. He also had to work hard when setting up the cabinet in a new place in connection with the relocation of the laboratory, which will be discussed below. With his own hands, Tsvet made a most interesting collection of 63 histological preparations of various tissues and organs of plants."
msgstr "Working in the Biological Laboratory directed by Lesgaft, Tsvet moved even further away from plant anatomy and switched his attention to plant physiology and biochemistry, to broader biological issues. This is evidenced by his interest in the effect of X-rays on the organism of plants and animals, in the comparative biochemistry of hemoglobin and chlorophyll in connection with the study of the common origin of plants and animals; even the results of his observations on plant anatomy, as we have seen, Tsvet began to present differently than before in similar Geneva publications."
msgstr "In 1897, Tsvet was the only botanist who used the services of the laboratory. In its report for this year it was stated: \"Mikhail Semyonovich Tsvet (Doctor of Botany of Geneva University) was engaged in studying the question of the significance of hemoglobin and chlorophyll and in general the anatomy and physiology of plants\" \\[24\\]. Few researchers worked in the departments of zoology and physiology. The charter of the laboratory allowed it to accept only fifteen experimenters that year. Among them, in particular, was Anna Alexandrovna Trachevskaya, who, like Tsvet, graduated from Geneva University and was engaged in the study of lower animals. Only two years later the situation changed somewhat and the number of those studying plant anatomy and physiology increased to 5 people (V.L. Komarov, A.I. Nabokikh, O.I. Nabokikh, L.V. Ochakovsky, D.F. Mazaraki), besides there was also a bacteriologist B.L. Isachenko \\[25\\]. A significant contribution to the activation of this work was made by Tsvet, who began to deploy it from the first steps of the activity of the Biological Laboratory."
msgstr "However, Lesgaft could not reconcile himself with the fact that a very limited number of people, and moreover those already having a higher education, study in the institution he opened. He transferred his home courses to the premises of the Biological Laboratory and provided an opportunity for all young men and women enrolled in them to use its cabinets. Striving to legitimize these courses and give them a greater scope, Lesgaft sought for a long time permission from the government for their official existence. With great difficulty, at the end of 1896, he managed to obtain such permission and open another institution under the lengthy name \"Courses for female educators and female leaders of physical education\"[^91.1]. At the beginning of the next year, Lesgaft began conducting classes in these courses. There were so many people wishing to study that the premises rented for the laboratory could not accommodate everyone. It was necessary to think about expanding it. At the end of 1897, the laboratory moved to a specially rebuilt house No. 25/6 for it on the corner of Torgovaya St. and Angliysky Prospect (now house 25a/32 on the corner of Soyuz Pechatnikov St. and Maklin Prospect)."
msgstr "The new building of the laboratory was subdivided into a natural history museum, the exposition of which consisted mainly of biological collections, a natural science library, and special rooms for practical classes. In addition, there were several rooms in the house for laboratory workers. Tsvet settled in one of them. He accepted Lesgaft's invitation to conduct botany classes with the female students, and then began to head the botanical department of the laboratory. At that time, it could hardly have occurred to anyone that three quarters of a century later a memorial plaque would be affixed to this building about the life and work in it of the creator of chromatography (see on p. 92), who first got a job here upon arriving in the homeland of his ancestors. Finally, after a year's stay in Petersburg, Tsvet was able to find, albeit a modest, but steady salary, which freed him from the material dependence on his father that so burdened him."
msgstr ""
msgstr "About his new duties and further plans for work in the Petersburg Biological Laboratory, Tsvet reported on October 10, 1897, to Geneva as follows: \"This winter I am reading a course in plant anatomy and physiology at the Petersburg Biological Laboratory, a new interesting institution. I also want to organize practical classes there. The course — of course, in Russian — gives me a fair amount of work... brings me anything you want... except chervontsy (gold coins). And besides, to top it all off, I have to prepare for this damned 'master's' exam. I can assure you that it is quite difficult for me to put back in order the layers of my systematic knowledge that have not been used for quite a long time.\""
msgstr "The need to read lectures in Russian, indeed, forced Tsvet to prepare for them especially carefully, since previously his spoken language was French. But he coped perfectly with this difficult task. A few months after the start of reading the course, Tsvet recalled his first lectures not without a smile: \"At first I had a lot of lapsus linguae (Latin — slips of the tongue. — E.S.),\" he wrote to N.I. Kuznetsov on January 31, 1898, \"and even very funny ones, for example, 'plastic diapers' (instead of plastids), 'seed of a mug' (instead of seed of rye)... Now it's nothing. A good school\" (cited from: Vaga A.Ya., 1958, p. 14). And once, instead of telling the female students: \"I observe in you a complete lack of order,\" he said: \"I observe in you a complete lack of decency\" (Lyashchenko E.A., 1970, p. 10)."
msgstr "Reading lectures in Russian was indeed a good school, having passed which, Tsvet perfectly mastered it two years later, as evidenced by his subsequent publications in Russian and the memoirs of contemporaries. Thus, the famous Soviet plant physiologist A.A. Richter, who attended his lectures, wrote: \"Tsvet was a good lecturer, and in his speeches he even had an element of a certain artistry. Occasionally he slipped typical for the French language stresses on the last syllable of a word: 'alfá', 'betá', but in general his Russian speech was pure, flawless, although he spent his childhood and youth in French Switzerland\" (Richter A.A., Krasnoselskaya T.A., 1946, p. 231)."
msgstr "The first year of Tsvet's stay in Petrograd was especially difficult not only materially. He felt no less acutely the absence of close friends and colleagues, in the choice of whom he was very strict. An example of this can be his attitude towards his cousin Dmitry Milievich Tsvet (1873—?) — a peer and partly a colleague. It was already mentioned above that Tsvet's uncle M.N. Tsvet, living in Petersburg, adopted an abandoned child and gave him his surname. D.M. Tsvet graduated from the prestigious Petersburg Gurevich Gymnasium (1890) and the Military Medical Academy (1896) with good grades, after which he worked at the VMA as a supernumerary assistant (1898), and then as a laboratory assistant (1907) \\[26\\], gravitating towards chemical and microbiological sciences. It would seem that not only kinship, but also a commonality of professional interests should have contributed to the rapprochement of Tsvet, who arrived in a city unfamiliar to him, with his cousin — a native Petersburger. Both of them were participants in the XI Congress of Naturalists and Physicians held in Petersburg (1901), at which M.S. Tsvet spoke with two reports at the botanical section (see M.S. Tsvet, 1902, Nos. 26, 28), and D.M. Tsvet as a doctor and member of the Russian Physico-Chemical Society took part in the work of the chemistry section \\[27\\]. In the same 1901, Dmitry Milievich opened a private laboratory for chemical-microscopic and sanitary-hygienic research \\[28\\], most likely at the expense of the inheritance from his father M.N. Tsvet, who had died by that time. It is possible that M.S. Tsvet could have helped D.M. Tsvet in setting up his laboratory, since he already had relevant experience in equipping his botanical cabinet. However, a close rapprochement between them did not happen. In his activities, D.M. Tsvet was guided by a certain merchant principle, which was alien to M.S. Tsvet. As the future showed, D.M. Tsvet indeed did not become a researcher, and the laboratory he opened acquired the character of a commercial enterprise, in no way similar, for example, to such a private scientific institution as Lesgaft's Biological Laboratory. This disunity was even greater in 1897, and friendship with such botanists as D.I. Ivanovsky and D.N. Nelyubov had not yet formed."
msgstr "At that time, Tsvet's closest friends still remained his Geneva colleagues. That is why he was especially glad to learn about the arrival in Moscow for a medical congress (August 1897) of Claparède and his wife Elena Afrikanovna, as already noted, of Russian origin. Advising \"not to harbor dark intentions to leave Russia without visiting Petersburg,\" Tsvet in a letter dated August 17, 1897, assured them that \"Petersburg is much more chic than Moscow, and besides there are surroundings — the famous Peterhof, which is more beautiful than Versailles, as the Russians say. And besides, damn it, there is me, to whom you will give great pleasure by spending a few days here\" \\[29\\]. In the same letter, he reported on his participation as a translator at the medical congress in Petersburg, on scientific trips with French participants around the city, and with special pleasure — on the upcoming small Russian-Geneva holiday."
msgstr "One can judge the further events of Tsvet's life in Petersburg and his moods from the scientist's letters to his colleagues and friends that have come down to us. In the mentioned message to N.I. Kuznetsov dated January 31, 1898, Tsvet reported: \"I now manage the botanical department in Lesgaft's biological laboratory and teach botany to female students symbiotizing with this laboratory\" (Cited from: Vaga A.Ya., 1958, p. 14)."
msgstr "At the same time, Tsvet worked intensely on his master's thesis — he studied the abundant literature on photosynthesis and chlorophyll, and also conducted experiments both in the academic Cabinet of Plant Anatomy and Physiology and in the Petersburg Biological Laboratory."
msgstr "In the latter, he also had to do a lot of work to organize better conditions for his own research, as well as for conducting practical classes with female students. Here, in particular, is the result of this work for 1898: \"During this year, a chemical laboratory for chemical-biological research was set up, and physiological and botanical departments were set up and expanded, new physical and biological instruments, microscopes and apparatuses for microscopic work were acquired\" \\[30\\]. One can judge what this equipment was from the same report: \"For the physiological and botanical cabinets, as well as for the chemical laboratory, during this year various instruments were ordered from O. Platt in Potsdam, J. Carpentier and A. Molteni in Paris. Microscopes were ordered from Hartnack in Potsdam and from Leitz in Wetzlar. In addition, microscopic instruments and glassware were received from Schroter and G. Grubler in Leipzig\" \\[31\\]."
msgstr "By 1898, Tsvet's position had significantly strengthened. The tone of his letters to his friend Briquet in Geneva also changed. Pessimism and disappointment disappeared from them. This is clearly evidenced by a comparison of two letters quoted below. Thus, after a two-week stay in Petersburg, Tsvet wrote on December 30, 1896: \"I came to a conclusion, very sad and discouraging. During the more than six months that I have been in Russia, I have been trying in vain to make myself feel that a Russian heart beats in my chest! I crossed all of Russia. I visited Moscow, the holy city, and my eyes and ears were wide open... Nothing trembled, nothing echoed in me. In my homeland I felt like a foreigner. And this feeling depresses me deeply and desperately. Now I am sorry that I left Europe; it would have been much better to ask Patzog for the position of assistant, which was vacant then. I assure you that if anything analogous presents itself now, I will not hesitate to return...\"."
msgstr "It was exactly this passage from Tsvet's letters that many foreign authors willingly quoted, keeping silent and not recalling another letter of the scientist, written a year and a half later, on August 27, 1898... where he refuses the offer passed through Briquet to work in Germany. \"Thank you for your offer, which a year ago I would have been glad to accept without hesitation,\" Tsvet wrote then to Briquet. \"However, now I have achieved a position here as good as the one you offer me, and in the near future it should improve even more. And moreover, I cannot bring myself to exchange my independence for duties, in all probability, heavy and requiring a lot of time. I understand well that a stay in the scientific world of Germany would give me a little of this 'school' — as they say here, in other words, that pedantry, that straightforwardness in work, which I lack a little. Well, so what, I will have to make up for this lack at the expense of my will.\""
msgstr "In the same letter, Tsvet drops the following phrase: \"We Russians, — from all points of view I can say 'we', because I have achieved that I have become quite like my compatriots...\". Perhaps the change in Tsvet's mood was also facilitated by a meeting with his father, who came to Petersburg during his vacation in June — July 1898. Two months later, S.N. Tsvet again came to Petersburg on vacation, after the end of which on October 11 he was \"left in St. Petersburg on official business\" \\[32\\] for another month. Most likely, the reason for this second trip was the death of Miliy Nikolaevich Tsvet — S.N. Tsvet's brother and godfather of V.S. Tsvet — Semyon Nikolaevich's youngest son. The inheritance affairs of the unmarried M.N. Tsvet required documentary formalization, in which S.N. Tsvet could assist. It is not excluded that the two nephews — V.S. Tsvet and M.S. Tsvet — could also have been bequeathed something by their uncle, a kind and responsive man, who sometimes helped the \"native foreigner\" in his difficult days of living at first in the unwelcoming Petersburg, which seemed especially gloomy after sunny Geneva. However, by the end of 1898, Tsvet was able to get acquainted not only with the cold and windy days of winter Petersburg, which almost did not see sunlight, but also with its summer white nights, which could not but bewitch his poetic nature. Another relative of Tsvet settled in this city — his uncle Grigory Nikolaevich Tsvet, and most importantly, friendly relations with colleagues were increasingly established, the circle of which was expanding, Tsvet's character — truthful and open, mild and delicate — involuntarily aroused sympathy for him from everyone who knew him closely."
msgstr "The significantly greater busyness of Tsvet in research and teaching work in 1898 is indicated by the very fact that the time gap with the previous letter to Briquet dated October 10, 1897, is almost a year. After many intense months of lectures, classes, and experiments in the laboratory, he with special pleasure got out to the environs of Petersburg and further to get acquainted with the flora new to him. Tsvet wrote to his Geneva friend about his impressions of some of such trips on October 10, 1897: \"This summer I spent several excursions in Finland and in the local environs. I found that interesting arctic bog flora, those picturesque landscapes that we went to look for in your Chablais in Roi-d'Enfer. White Ledum, elegant Oxycoccus, and further Linnaea borealis, sung by Correvon. Two h\\[ours drive\\] from P\\[etersburg\\], in Pargolovo, there is a small sleeping lake, hills covered with forest. This area is called 'Russian Switzerland'. On this small lake grows a rare plant Lobelia Dortsmannes — a bouquet of thread-like submerged leaves and flowers peeking above the surface of the water.\""
msgstr "The following summer he managed to visit Crimea again on the seashore, obviously to bolster his health. \"I spent two summer months in Sudak (a place on the southern coast of Crimea) as a French teacher for two boys,\" Tsvet wrote about this trip to Briquet in the same letter. \"The rare leisure time that I had left, I used to excursion in the mountains and collect the most interesting specimens of the magnificent xerophilous flora. I collected half a dozen Labiatae, and at the first convenient opportunity I will send you duplicates. And now here I am again taking on double duties, which consist, on the one hand, in teaching scientia amabilis (Latin beloved science. — E.S.) in a women's institute, and on the other hand, in directing practical work in botany in a biological laboratory — a recently emerged private institution. Of course, I do not forget the university either, but beforehand I must pass the exam and present my dissertation.\""
msgstr "When preparing for the master's exam, Tsvet was also worried about the question of the place of taking it. At Petersburg University, such an exam was taken only by the head of the botanical department, Professor Kh.Ya. Gobi — a specialist in lower plants, who, according to the testimony of his students and contemporaries, in accordance with his specialty, made excessive demands on exams in mycology and algology. Since Tsvet had never worked in these areas of botany, he could not count on a positive outcome of the exam. Therefore, on January 31, 1898, he turned for consultation to the professor of Yuryev (until 1893 — Dorpat, and since 1919 — Tartu) University Nikolai Ivanovich Kuznetsov (1864—1932) with the following letter: \"Highly respected Monsieur Kuznetsov! I appeal to you with a humble request to enlighten me on the following points. Would Dorpat University allow me to take the master's exam directly (I have a doctor's diploma from Geneva and a certificate of maturity from there too — a real gymnasium)? In what subjects would I have to be examined and what are the general requirements? Is it possible to present a dissertation written in French, or a Russian summary of it? I intend to take it by autumn, after the holidays, the dissertation will be ready already in the spring\" (cited from: Vaga A.Ya., 1958, pp. 13—14)."
msgstr "Tsvet was acquainted with Kuznetsov from the circle of \"little botanists,\" to the meeting of which Kuznetsov, for old times' sake, came from Yuryev, where in 1895 he received a professorship at Yuryev University. Therefore, Tsvet wrote to him in a friendly and joking manner, which can be judged at least by the concluding message in the letter about a funny incident that occurred on the pages of one of the German botanical journals: \"Mot de la fin\" (French concluding word. — E.S.); the former French minister Baihaut (a Panamist), who was convicted and imprisoned, recently published a book in which he describes his prison impressions under the title \"Impressions cellulaires\" (French prison impressions. — E.S.) This book is classified as botanical literature! See Botan. Central\\[blatt\\] (An abstract botanical journal published in Germany. — E.S.) of this year No. 3... Vive les Allemands! (French Long live the Germans! — E.S.)\" (Cited from: Vaga A.Ya., 1958, p. 14). Lively and spontaneous, Tsvet even in a business letter could not help but joke, having encountered the funny incident of the play on words \"cell\" (in the biological sense) and \"cell\", \"chamber\" (in the sense of a prison room) when translating the French concept \"cellule\"."
msgstr "The fact that Kuznetsov carefully considered Tsvet's request is evidenced by brief records of the list of subjects outlined by Kuznetsov for Tsvet to pass the master's exam, preserved in the manuscript department of the Tartu University library. However, before giving him a final answer, Kuznetsov tried to find out in more detail from Famintsyn the circumstances related to Tsvet's request."
msgstr "We were unable to find Kuznetsov's letter (it is not in A.S. Famintsyn's fund of the Archive of the Russian Academy of Sciences), however, Famintsyn's answer has been preserved: \"Highly respected Nikolai Ivanovich! In response to your inquiry regarding Tsvet, I consider it my duty to report the following: he has been studying in my laboratory for a short time and is finishing, without the slightest interference on my part, his work on plant anatomy, the title of which I do not remember exactly. I know only two of his works, small ones, in my opinion, presenting nothing special and, in my opinion, not giving him the right to take the master's exam, since he, having been educated abroad, does not have a certificate of maturity. The best thing you can do is to demand that he send you his scientific works: look for yourself and decide. To the second question: why doesn't he take the exam in Petersburg — the answer is simple: only Gobi examines everything\" (Cited from: Vaga A.Ya., 1958, p. 15)."
msgstr "Let us pay attention to Famintsyn's last remark, without which it would be impossible to understand the reason for such a turn in Tsvet's life as the forced search for a place to take the master's exam and defend his dissertation outside Petersburg, namely in the universities of Yuryev and Kazan, where, by the way, now by the vicissitudes of fate there are memorial plaques associated with his name. The reason for this was the professor of Petersburg University Khristofor Yakovlevich Gobi (1847—1919), who headed the department of morphology and systematics of lower plants since 1879 and dealt mainly with algae of the northern seas. Since A.S. Famintsyn left the University back in 1889, and A.N. Beketov, who headed the department of morphology and systematics of flowering plants, was paralyzed in the summer of 1897, Gobi united both named departments and became the only professor of botany at Petersburg University."
msgstr "According to the testimony of the famous historian of biology B.E. Raikov, who took a botanical course with him, Gobi \"was a good scientist, but possessed no teaching abilities. His lectures were so dry and boring that students attended them extremely poorly and expressed no interest in botany,\" having composed the saying: \"From the Neva to the Ob there is no one more boring than Gobi\" \\[18, p. 29\\]. The same characteristic is given by the historian of botany A.A. Shcherbakova: \"Gobi's lectures were nicknamed 'gobistics', they served their turn in attending them, composed ironic poems about him\" \\[33, p. 36\\]. Passing the botanical exam to him was the most difficult and unpleasant task."
msgstr "Even Gobi's close student N.M. Gaidukov, who highly appreciated the teacher's teaching talent, could not help but note Gobi's manner of taking exams, unusual for students, and the possibility of getting a good grade at the same time. \"... At these exams,\" Gaidukov recalled, \"the voice of the examiner was heard more often than the examinee. The test did not consist in the examinee's answers showing the scope of his knowledge. No. These were scientific conversations, during which Gobi, after posing a question, himself became inspired and developed the train of thought... If Gobi noticed that the examinee could follow the course of these thoughts and himself became inspired, then he gave him a positive assessment\" \\[34\\]."
msgstr "According to Raikov and Shcherbakova, Gobi was also particularly unfriendly to talented people with independent views[^99.1]. Such an attitude of his extended to colleagues of both the older generation (in particular, A.S. Famintsyn, I.P. Borodin, etc.) and younger ones (future academician V.L. Komarov, V.V. Polovtsov, etc.), especially if among the latter there were novice botanists who enjoyed the favor of Academician Famintsyn and the opportunity to experiment in his laboratory of plant anatomy and physiology. It is understandable that Tsvet could in no way count on obtaining a master's degree in botany with Gobi's participation. Famintsyn also understood this when he answered N.I. Kuznetsov's inquiry."
msgstr "At the same time, as can be seen from the letter, Famintsyn particularly emphasized his non-interference in Tsvet's research. The statement corresponded to the truth and was all the more necessary because the applicant for an academic degree was required not only to independently perform the work, but even to personally choose the topic of the dissertation. As for Tsvet's anatomical work mentioned in the letter, later published under the title \"On the connection of external and internal leptomes of Solanaceae\" (Tswett M., No. 13, 1899), it was indeed carried out without the interference of Famintsyn, who was not engaged in research in this area. The first two articles in Russian prepared for publication at that time (Tsvet M.S., 1898, No. 8; 1899, No. 9) had the character of critical reviews and, of course, could not serve as an application for a dissertation. It could only be his previous Geneva botanical publications in French, with which Famintsyn suggested Kuznetsov familiarize himself. Obviously, the restrained attitude in this letter of Famintsyn to Tsvet's works, and especially the remark that Tsvet's latest works in Russian do not give him the right to take exams for a master's degree, prompted Kuznetsov, without familiarizing himself with previous publications, to answer Tsvet with a refusal. Note that while treating his younger colleagues benevolently and helping them, Famintsyn was at the same time very demanding of the results of their work, but always approved and supported interesting research, including Tsvet's master's thesis."
msgstr "Unfortunately, in connection with the cited letter of Famintsyn, a shadow was cast on him without sufficient grounds in one of the publications of K.I. Sakodynsky. According to the latter, the restraint of Famintsyn's review of Tsvet's works until 1898 can allegedly be explained by Famintsyn's dissatisfaction, who could have become aware of Tsvet's message in a letter to Briquet dated December 30, 1896, that in the academician's experiments on isolating chloroplasts, \"it turns out there is no success to date\" (Sakodynsky K.I., 1972, p. 19). In the presented translation of Tsvet's letter, it turns out that he was surprised and even ironized about the fruitlessness of Famintsyn's work. In reality, Tsvet wrote: \"Mr. Famintsyn has been dealing for ten years with the problem that I set myself when starting my dissertation: to make chloroplasts live in an isolated culture. To date, he has not yet succeeded in doing this\" (italics ours. — E.S.); let us add from ourselves, as Tsvet and dozens of other researchers up to our days also failed to do."
msgstr "With this message, Tsvet in no way questioned the scientific validity of Famintsyn's experiments, but only reported on the complexity of the problem they were both studying and the commonality of their scientific interests, which could in no way cause Famintsyn's dissatisfaction, even if he had learned about the content of the letter addressed by Tsvet to Briquet, with whom, by the way, Famintsyn did not maintain any connection. Sakodynsky's concept also contradicts Tsvet's statements about his great gratitude to Famintsyn not only \"for the generous provision of the means of his laboratory\" for work on his master's thesis, but also \"for that full of high tact spiritual assistance that the venerable physiologist renders to workers in his hospitable institute\" (M.S. Tsvet, 1901, No. 23, p. 1). Later, Tsvet, upon Famintsyn's review, received an academic prize, was recommended to occupy a chair at Novorossiysk University, and their good relations are best evidenced by Tsvet's letter quoted on pp. 103—104. Thus, Sakodynsky's opinion seems unfounded."
msgstr "In 1899, Tsvet successfully passed the master's exam in Kazan. The message that \"M.S. Tsvet passed the exam for the degree of Master of Botany at the Imp. Kazan University\" \\[35\\] appeared in the \"Proceedings of the Botanical Garden of Yuryev University\" only two years later, when Tsvet's already published work \"Physico-chemical structure of the chlorophyll grain\" (1901) was accepted for defense as a master's thesis. In connection with this message, he sent to Yuryev on August 30, 1901, to Kuznetsov as the executive editor of the \"Proceedings\" a letter in French of the following content: \"Dear Sir! The Yuryev botanical journal reports that I passed the master's exams in Kazan in the spring. Indeed, I remember that I recently fulfilled these formalities to please the protectionism reigning in our country, but it was two years ago. If the organ of Yuryev botanists considers it necessary to note events that have no significance, could it not be done with greater accuracy? Accept, dear sir, the assurance of my perfect respect for you. M. Tsvet Doctor of Sciences\" (cited from: Vaga A.Ya., 1958, p. 16)."
msgstr ""
msgstr "A.Ya. Vaga, who discovered this letter in the manuscript department of the Tartu University library, published it and accompanied it with a commentary: \"In this letter there is nothing friendly and comradely anymore,\" noted Vaga, comparing it with Tsvet's message to Kuznetsov on January 31, 1898. \"The question is, however, why did M.S. Tsvet consider it necessary to write it in French? After all, now, after four years of work in Russia, it was no longer difficult for him to write in Russian. It can hardly be considered a reason that the French language allows expressing one's disapproval to the addressee in a polite form, in refined expressions, without insulting him, but quite definitely. It seems more likely that this was done in order to particularly emphasize the main idea of the letter, namely, that M. Tsvet considers passing exams as an empty formality. Under the French letter, one could sign as a Doctor of Sciences of Geneva University, emphasizing by this that although he is forced to take the master's exam, he himself considers himself and suggests others consider him a Doctor of Sciences\" (ibid., pp. 16—17)."
msgstr "It is unknown whether Kuznetsov's answer to Tsvet followed. However, this exchange of letters did not lead to discord between their authors, since the \"Proceedings of the Botanical Garden of Yuryev University\" continued to print reports on the successes of Tsvet's scientific activity in the future, and he, in turn, published in them in 1911 his review of V.K. Kononov's work \"A Brief Outline of Plant Anatomy\" (St. Petersburg, 1911). Obviously, this was the case when the ironic tone of the letter did not at all testify to the author's hostility towards the addressee; between friends such a tone is permissible, and their good relations do not suffer from this."
msgstr "Tsvet's preparation for the exam and for writing his master's thesis went on without interruption from his intense pedagogical work at Lesgaft's courses. On September 30, 1898, he reported to Geneva: \"This year, second-year female students are working in my laboratory. One of them is studying the effect of anesthetics on plasmolysis. As for me, I am still enthusiastically investigating the structure of chlorophyll; however it goes further, already now as a result of research I have a lot of new data. For the rest, I have relatively little time left for myself. In the mornings I conduct laboratory classes with 75 girls... whom I initiate into plant anatomy, add to them the second-year students and the need to prepare for classes: you see, my work is not a sinecure (from Lat. without care — in the meaning of a position that does not require much labor, but gives significant income. — E.S.)\"."
msgstr "As more and more interesting results of studying chlorophyll in the plant accumulated, Tsvet took their discussion beyond the circle of \"little botanists.\" On December 15, 1899, he made a presentation \"On chloroglobin\" at a meeting of the botanical department of the Petersburg Society of Naturalists. The presentation, as well as Tsvet's publication on the same topic that appeared shortly after, caused a critical speech by N.A. Monteverde (1900). On April 19, 1900, at a meeting of the society, Tsvet made a response report \"On the nature of chloroglobin,\" in which he defended his point of view on the content of chlorophyll in living plants in the form of chloroglobin. Another report by Tsvet, \"Chlorophyllins and metachlorophyllins,\" made six months later in the same place, was discussed no less actively."
msgstr "Speeches at the Petersburg Society of Naturalists, as well as publications that had come out of print by that time, allowed Tsvet to take a fitting place among the \"big botanists\"; on April 19, 1900, M.S. Voronin, I.P. Borodin, and D.I. Ivanovsky (A.S. Famintsyn was absent from the meeting due to illness) recommended Tsvet as a full member of this society, and on December 3, 1900, their proposal was approved by the general meeting of the society. N.I. Kuznetsov, who was present at the meeting, this time did not object to Tsvet defending his master's thesis at Yuryev University, but Tsvet had already agreed on this with the botany department of Kazan University."
msgstr "In October 1900, Petersburg botanists solemnly celebrated the 65th birthday and the 40th anniversary of the scientific activity of A.S. Famintsyn. Numerous students of the first Russian phytophysiologist presented the hero of the anniversary with an album with their photographs, which is now kept in the Institute of Plant Physiology of the Russian Academy of Sciences, originating from the laboratory of plant anatomy and physiology of A.S. Famintsyn. Among these photographs is also placed a portrait of Tsvet (see insert — p. V) with his dedicatory inscription on the back of the portrait \"To the deeply respected Andrei Sergeevich on the day of the fortieth anniversary of his truly unselfish and glorious scientific activity. October 14, 1900.\""
msgstr "Later, on June 17, 1915, when the entire scientific world will celebrate the 80th anniversary of the head of Russian phytophysiologists, Tsvet will address A.S. Famintsyn with sincere words of gratitude: \"... on this day,\" he wrote, \"I once again remember how 20 years ago, having returned from a foreign land and a stranger to everyone, I found with you a scientific haven and a high example — an example of a life devoted truly to science, as a goal, and not just as a means of personal well-being, an example of scientific original activity, stubbornly pursuing the solution of conceived tasks, not embarrassed by surrounding currents, silence or hype, an example of that scientific gentlemanliness, benevolence towards other scientific workers, which has become so rare among the younger generation! For all this I bring you once again my gratitude and am proud that to some extent I can be counted among your students\" \\[36\\]. Unselfish service to science, tireless search for new paths in it, adherence to principles in achieving set scientific goals, honesty, modesty, and benevolence — all this became integral properties of Tsvet's personality itself."
msgstr "The intense work of 1899—1900, which can be judged by 14 articles published by Tsvet during this time in Russian and foreign journals, received its completion and generalization in his detailed manuscript \"Physico-chemical structure of the chlorophyll grain\" (1901). In the midst of preparing it for publication, sad news came from Yalta about the death of his father. Tsvet took this news hard. His father was for him not only a model of high morality, but also the first helper, an adviser in difficult days. Tsvet was burdened by the realization that he would no longer be able to help his father now, when such an opportunity after defending his dissertation was becoming quite real."
msgstr ""
msgstr "And the last years of S.N. Tsvet's life were not easy. He was often ill, but due to lack of funds could not leave the service. \"If I were alone,\" S.N. Tsvet wrote to his Petersburg colleague on April 8, 1899, \"without further ado I would go quietly to the grave, — but at home a wife, little ones, they have neither a stake nor a yard. Willy-nilly, one must take care of maintaining health for the service, while one has the strength\" \\[37\\]. Despite his illness and busyness at work, N.S. Tsvet was still drawn to literary work. In the summer of 1897, he negotiated with the Petersburg publisher of the journal \"Istorichesky Vestnik\" S.N. Shubinsky about republishing his brochure \"Russia and England in the Eastern Question,\" since he considered \"relations with England a vital question for our entire future\" \\[38\\]. However, due to deteriorating health, he had to stop his journalistic pursuits, and in January 1899 completely leave the service. Having received a pension, S.N. Tsvet moved with his family to Yalta. However, he did not have to live in this city for long. The pneumonia that followed three months later ended tragically \\[39\\]. In the Sunday issue of the local newspaper \"Krymsky Kuryer\" on April 30, 1900, it was reported: \"On the night of the 29th of this April, the former manager of the Taurida Treasury Chamber, Actual State Councilor Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet, died at Ostroumov's dacha.\" He was buried in Yalta at the Ioanno-Zlatoustinskoye cemetery. On the monument of red marble was carved the inscription: \"Act. st. couns. Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet, d. 29 Apr. 1900 Yal. I-Zl\" \\[40\\]. The monument has not survived to our days, but one can judge its majesty from a preserved photograph."
msgstr "A tribute of gratitude to his father was the dedication of M.S. Tsvet's master's thesis, on a separate page of which it was stated: \"Dedicated to the memory of my father — thinker and doer — Semyon Nikolaevich Tsvet\" (see p. 106), without whose help this work, perhaps, would not have been done. In difficult days of doubt, Semyon Nikolaevich helped his son not only materially, but also morally, when the latter had the thought of leaving Russia. M.S. Tsvet forever retained a fond memory of his father; he greatly valued Semyon Nikolaevich's large notebook preserved by him, which contained various literary notes and reasoning."
msgstr "At the beginning of 1901, Tsvet's dissertation work saw the light in the \"Proceedings of the Society of Naturalists at Kazan University\"[^106.1], and in the first days of April the scientist appealed to the rector of Kazan University with a request to accept it for defense. On April 16, the dissertation was handed over to the head of the botanical department N.V. Sorokin[^106.2] so that he would give \"a written analysis of the essay by Mr. Tsvet 'Physico-chemical structure of the chlorophyll grain' attached hereto, presented by him as a dissertation for the degree of Master of Botany\" \\[41\\]."
msgstr ""
msgstr "On the title page of the dissertation, Tsvet signed as a Doctor of Geneva University and right there gave its title in French, as if emphasizing the continuity between the dissertation completed in Geneva and the dissertation now being defended in Kazan. He always valued the academic degree of Doctor of Geneva University and, when signing, always indicated it, even in the last years of his life, when he had already become a Doctor of Botany in Russia."
msgstr "In a brief preface to his master's thesis, Tsvet wrote about the goals and conditions of its execution as follows: \"The proposed work contains the results of my three years of research on some important for the physiologist questions of the physics and chemistry of the chlorophyll grain. What is offered here is not a synthetic monograph — our science has not yet matured for the compilation of such — but a series of essays, in which the author attempts to shed the light of a more rational experiment on some controversial, delicate aspects of the question."
msgstr "The research, begun in the Petersburg Biological Laboratory during my time there as a lecturer, was carried out mostly in the botanical laboratory of the Academy of Sciences. I consider it a pleasant duty to express here my gratitude to the highly respected Academician Andrei Sergeevich Famintsyn for the generous provision of the means of his laboratory to me, as well as for that full of high tact spiritual assistance that the venerable physiologist renders to those working in his hospitable institute\" (M.S. Tsvet, 1901, No. 23, p. 1)."
msgstr "N.V. Sorokin quite quickly familiarized himself with the dissertation that interested him and already by the beginning of May presented a review of it, which was published in the same year in the \"Scientific Notes of Kazan University.\" At the very beginning of his review, the reviewer noted the fact that the study of chlorophyll began in 1791, when A. Comparetti discovered chlorophyll grains in plant leaves. \"Since then,\" he continued, \"these formations have not ceased to interest scientific researchers, and the literature on chlorophyll has grown to enormous proportions. But, despite the seemingly most accurate methods, our knowledge of the structure and physiological role of leaf green remains still very meager in many respects. Mr. Tsvet undertook to shed the light of a more rational experiment on some controversial, delicate or even completely untouched aspects of the question\" (Sorokin N.V., 1901, p. 1)."
msgstr "Having thoroughly covered the content of Tsvet's work and evaluated its individual parts, Sorokin made the following conclusion: \"From all that has been said, one can conclude about the painstaking labor of Mr. Tsvet. To decide to start studying any, even a particular question about chlorophyll, can only a person who selflessly loves science, who is not frightened by the huge literary material and who is thoroughly prepared for such work."
msgstr "Mr. Tsvet's research led him to the discovery of new interesting compounds enclosed in chloroplasts, and a critical assessment of previous and modern literature proves his solid erudition. Therefore, I can state without hesitation that the essay entitled 'Physico-chemical structure of the chlorophyll grain' completely satisfies the requirements that can be presented to it for seeking the degree of Master of Botany.\""
msgstr "On May 6, Tsvet's work and N.V. Sorokin's review of it were handed over to all members of the physics and mathematics faculty. However, due to the upcoming defense in May of the master's thesis of the botanist A.Ya. Gordyagin, Tsvet's future opponent, the dispute on Tsvet's dissertation work was scheduled for the autumn."
msgstr "Tsvet spent the summer of 1901 with his relatives — his stepmother, sisters, and younger brother — whom he helped move from Yalta to Odessa after his father's death; his uncle Ivan Nikolaevich Tsvet lived there at that time. At the same time, M.S. Tsvet had to take care of his brother Alexander, who, after graduating from the Melitopol Real School in the summer of 1900, came to his brother in Petersburg to enter the institute. In August, Alexander passed the competitive exams and became a student of the Mining Institute of Empress Catherine II, but he did not study there for long. On February 27, 1901, he submitted a petition to the Institute for dismissal due to health reasons[^108.1] with the right of return admission to the same course. He did not have a chance to return to this Institute. Fate connected A.S. Tsvet with naval service, which will be discussed below."
msgstr "Meanwhile, preparations were underway at Kazan University for the defense of M.S. Tsvet's master's thesis. Three days before the defense of the dissertation, the members of the Academic Council of Kazan University were handed a notification of the following content: \"I have the honor to inform the gentlemen professors and privat-docents of the physics and mathematics faculty,\" reported the dean of this faculty F.M. Suvorov, \"that on Sunday, September 23 at 1 pm in the assembly hall of the university there will be a public meeting of the physics and mathematics faculty, at which the Doctor of Geneva University M.S. Tsvet will defend the essay presented by him for the degree of Master of Botany entitled 'Physico-chemical structure of the chlorophyll grain' 1901, September 19\" \\[43\\]."
msgstr "The defense of the dissertation took place at the appointed time — September 23, 1901.[^108.2] Not only the named work was presented for discussion, but also eight theses, or, as they were then called, provisions, not directly related to the topic of the dissertation and testifying to the degree of scientific erudition and critical thinking of the dissertator. In them, Tsvet expressed his attitude to many issues of biology and his understanding of such problems as vitalism and mechanism in biology, the formulation of the study of chemistry, the connection between evolution and physiology, the relationship between systematics and classification of plants, Pfeffer's theory of the secondary assimilation curve, Timiryazev's explanation of the reasons for the failure to detect the maximum of photosynthesis in the yellow rays of the spectrum, etc. These theses were not printed together with the dissertation. They owe their publication to the \"Proceedings of the Botanical Garden of Yuryev University\" (see M.S. Tsvet, 1902, No. 25)"
msgstr "Besides the aforementioned N.V. Sorokin, the official opponents at the dispute were the honored ordinary professor of the department of agronomy Vasily Ivanovich Sorokin and the privat-docent of the department of plant systematics Andrei Yakovlevich Gordyagin, who shortly before had brilliantly defended his master's thesis on the soils and vegetation of Western Siberia, for the high merits of which he was immediately awarded the degree of Doctor of Botany. As unofficial opponents, professors D.A. Goldgrammer and S.M. Smirnov, as well as a student of the mathematical faculty, took part in the dispute."
msgstr "As Gordyagin's student V.I. Baranov later testified, at this dispute \"he (i.e., Gordyagin — E.S.) was the most active opponent together with N.V. Sorokin and V.I. Sorokin...\" (Baranov V.I., 1933, p. 15)."
msgstr "The speech of the dissertator himself was especially remembered by those present at the dissertation dispute. Based on the stories of eyewitnesses, T.A. Krasnoselskaya described this meeting as follows: \"This defense remained in the memory of those who attended it for a long time. Besides the fact that it went perfectly in essence, M.S. Tsvet fully manifested at it his inherent talent of a brilliant lecturer, who loved and knew how to masterfully stage his speech. His whole manner of holding himself was artistic, and by the general elation of the mood, the meeting of the faculty looked more like the celebration of an anniversary than the defense of a dissertation\" (Krasnoselskaya T.A., 1948, p. 844)."
msgstr "In the minutes of the meeting, however, it was briefly recorded. \"Upon the conclusion of the dispute, the faculty decided: to recognize the defense by Mr. Tsvet of the named dissertation as satisfactory, to award it the degree of Master of Botany, and to submit it to the University Council for approval\" \\[44\\]."
msgstr "As soon as Tsvet received notification of the approval by the University Council of the decision of the physics and mathematics faculty, he immediately applied on September 27, 1901, to the dean of this faculty with a request to include him in the number of privat-docents of Kazan University. This request was caused primarily by the fact that the Biological Laboratory temporarily curtailed its work. The reason was the events that took place in Petersburg in the spring of 1901. The news of the conscription of Kyiv, and then Petersburg students into soldiers for university unrest caused a stormy demonstration of youth from various institutes on Kazan Square on March 4, 1901."
msgstr "The police and Cossacks mercilessly cracked down on the demonstrators. On the initiative of Lesgaft, who witnessed this reprisal, 99 professors and writers wrote a protest to the government against the brutal violence against the youth. As a result, Lesgaft was exiled from Petersburg for two years to Terijoki (Finland), and his laboratory almost ceased its work."
msgstr "Thus, the assertions that Tsvet's departure from Lesgaft's courses was caused by the fact that \"the ambitious Tsvet, with big plans, thirsted for space to fulfill them\" (Ivin M.E., 1971, p. 161) or the repetition of the same thought that \"the young scientist is ambitious enough, he feels the calling of a researcher, he feels the aroma of discoveries that he has to make\" (Sakodynsky K.I., 1972, p. 24) are incorrect. In Petersburg, where Tsvet had already made his main discoveries, and developed the basis for their further development, there was more \"space for the fulfillment of big plans\" than in Kazan and Warsaw, where he was heading in search of a new place of work."
msgstr "At that time, the botany department of Kazan University was in great need of a plant physiologist, but the situation at the department was quite complicated. In the 70s—80s, N.V. Sorokin was the only botany teacher at the university; only in 1889 did Vladislav Adolfovich Rotert (1863—1916), a Master of Dorpat University, a Pole by nationality, who had recently taken Russian citizenship, receive an appointment to Kazan. Very soon disagreements arose regarding their duties between the new privat-docent and the head of the department N.V. Sorokin, who enjoyed the direct support of the royal family. According to the testimony of contemporaries, \"Sorokin was a vivid product of the old landlord environment, which did not accustom one to regular work... He could work, but either to satisfy his own interest, or to get additional earnings. Sorokin evaded even reading lectures to first-year students whenever possible, despite his ability to speak well...\" \\[45\\]. A different opinion soon formed about the new botany teacher: \"Rotert, a man of Western European upbringing and education, was a tireless worker and an excellent scientist, who left a very lasting mark in science."
msgstr "Possessing a highly developed ability for self-criticism, he was in this respect the exact opposite of Sorokin. It was worth learning from Rotert, following his instructions and advice, especially since these instructions never turned into unappealable guidance: being himself a person intellectually independent, Rotert highly valued the manifestations of independent thought in others, including his students\" \\[46\\]."
msgstr "Due to the aggravation of relations with Sorokin, Rotert was forced to leave Kazan for Kharkov in 1897, and the conduct of almost the entire botanical course fell on the shoulders of the still very young and inexperienced in the field of plant physiology privat-docent A.Ya. Gordyagin. Wanting to help his successor, Rotert, together with Gordyagin, made an attempt to attract someone from the Petersburg botanists to Kazan University, but it remained fruitless."
msgstr "Gordyagin took the conduct of the course on plant physiology very responsibly, conscientiously prepared for lectures and conducting practical classes, sometimes seriously getting carried away with experiments, as was the case with the study of luminous bacteria and winter evaporation of plants. However, still his main research interests lay in the field of plant geography and soil science, the result of which was the first soil map of the Kazan province, and then of all Western Siberia, so highly appreciated by scientists. Wanting to continue research in this direction and free himself from too much teaching load, Gordyagin supported Tsvet's intention to join the work of their department."
msgstr "On October 6, 1901, i.e., on the day when the University Council approved the faculty's decision to award Tsvet the academic degree of Master of Botany, he again applied to the dean of the physics and mathematics faculty with a request to issue him a certificate for the title of privat-docent for the right to read lectures on the topic \"History of fertilization in the plant kingdom in connection with the issues of heredity and variability of species.\" On October 15, 1901, the faculty decided: \"To petition for the admission of Mr. Tsvet to the number of privat-docents of the faculty and for permission for him to read the course indicated by him from the beginning of the next semester\" \\[47\\]."
msgstr ""
msgstr "Ten days later, such a petition was sent to the manager of the Kazan educational district. It contained an explanation: \"On the part of the head of the botanical cabinet, honored ordinary professor N.V. Sorokin, there are no obstacles for Mr. Tsvet to use the educational and auxiliary means and the cabinet\" \\[48\\]. What the manager's decision was on this petition is unknown. Most likely, Tsvet's appointment did not take place for a reason depending on himself."
msgstr "After submitting applications to Kazan University, he became aware of his election on November 26, 1901, by competition to the position of assistant of the department of plant anatomy and physiology at Warsaw University. This department was headed by D.I. Ivanovsky, who knew him well from work in Petersburg — a very benevolent person, demanding of himself and his work, responsive and delicate. Fearing that at his new place of service in Kazan he might develop bad relations with the head of the department, as happened with Rotert, many character traits of whom were also characteristic of Tsvet, the latter preferred to work as an assistant in Warsaw."
msgstr "Back in September 1901, the physics and mathematics faculty of Kazan University, in agreement with the Ministry of Education, began discussing the issue of expanding the staff and on December 1, 1901, initiated a petition \"on the introduction of a third professorship in the department of botany for teaching plant anatomy and physiology\" \\[49\\]. The possibility of Tsvet obtaining a professorial position at Kazan University was quite real. However, he accepted the offer of Warsaw University without hesitation also because there was already a well-equipped laboratory for plant physiology for research work, while at Kazan University it still had to be created. Tsvet, on the other hand, sought to complete as soon as possible the series of experiments he was conducting to test the adsorption method. He did not claim a professorial position in Kazan even when a competition was announced for it, but entirely immersed himself in experimental and pedagogical work as a supernumerary laboratory assistant at Warsaw University. This is another evidence of the insolvency of the aforementioned version about Tsvet's ambitious aspirations when moving to Warsaw."
msgstr "Before leaving for Warsaw, Tsvet, together with Ivanovsky and Khmelevsky, took part in the XI Congress of Russian Naturalists and Physicians, held in Petersburg on December 20—30, 1901. Tsvet participated very actively in the work of the congress, as evidenced by his repeated speeches on various issues. Thus, on December 23, he made two presentations — \"Anatomical study of saxaul (Haloxylon Ammoderdron)\" and \"Microtechnical notes.\" The first of them remained unpublished, and a brief summary of the second was printed in the \"Diary of the Congress\" (see Tsvet M.S., 1902, No. 28). The \"Microtechnical notes\" related to the technique of preparing cytological preparations and included three provisions: a recommendation to use a solution of resorcinol in water as a new clearing reagent; a high assessment of the \"Geneva reagent\" — a double dye for cell walls; and a description of the methodology for preparing empty cells, i.e., cells devoid of reserve substances (starch, fats, proteins, etc.), for microchemical research."
msgstr "The most notable was Tsvet's report on December 30, 1901, \"Methods and tasks of physiological research of chlorophyll\" (Tsvet M.S., 1902, No. 26), in which he first reported on his use of the phenomenon of adsorption to separate a mixture of plant pigments."
msgstr "Thus ended the Petersburg period of Tsvet's activity, a difficult period of searching, which determined the main direction of his further scientific searches — the improvement of the adsorption method for obtaining pure forms of plant pigments to clarify their biochemical nature."

msgstr "N.A. Maksimov. D.I. Ivanovsky as a plant physiologist // Mikrobiologiya. 1950, vol. 19, issue 6, pp. 499—505."

msgstr "Ivan Parfenyevich Borodin (1847—1930) — professor of botany at a number of Petersburg higher educational institutions, member of the Petersburg Academy of Sciences since 1902. His master's thesis (1876) is devoted to the study of plant respiration, and subsequent works — to the study of chlorophyll and plant anatomy. Organizer of the Russian Botanical Society in St. Petersburg at the Academy of Sciences (1916)."
msgstr "For more details see D.N. Nelyubov. Laboratory on plant anatomy and physiology of the Academy of Sciences // Chelovek i priroda, 1925, No. 9, pp. 39—47; and also Kleshnin A.F. et al., 1967."

Ivanovsky see: Maksimov N.A., 1947; Maksimov N.A. and Kalmykov K.F., 1954; Shcherbakova A.A. et al., 1957; Kalmykov K.F., 1962; Kleshnin A.F. et al., 1967; Bazilevskaya N.A. et al., 1968; Shcherbakova A.A. et al., 1983; as well as a number of special publications about this scientist \\[4—8\\]."

msgstr "Academician Mikhail Stepanovich Voronin (1838—1903) — botanist-mycologist and phytopathologist, from 1898 headed the Department of Cryptogamic Plants of the Petersburg Academy of Sciences. In the Petersburg Society of Naturalists he created a botanical department, which he headed until the end of his life. Honorary member of a number of domestic and foreign societies, including since 1896 — a foreign member of the Royal Society of London."
msgstr "Alexander Fyodorovich Batalin (1847—1896) — plant physiologist and florist-systematist, director of the Petersburg Botanical Garden in 1892—1896."
msgstr "Nikolai Avgustinovich Monteverde (1856—1929) — plant physiologist and specialist in applied botany, employee of the Petersburg Botanical Garden, since 1892 — in the position of head of the museum and physiological laboratory."
msgstr "Interesting data on the history of the creation of the circle is contained in the letter of the \"little botanists\" to A.N. Beketov in connection with the anniversary of their association. Here is an excerpt from this letter: \"Our circle is closely connected with you. After all, it originated with you, in your family! Some of us still remember those glorious evenings when the youth gathered at your place, in the circle of your hospitable family, at the tea table, encouraged by you, launched into lively scientific conversations and debates. It was these evenings that actually gave the impetus to the rapprochement of the youth among themselves, and in the end led one of your students to the good idea of establishing this circle 'Little Botanists'. We all regret that the founder of the circle is not present. But A.N. Krasnov was only the organizer of the circle; you created it, and we reap what you sowed, everyone has long been accustomed to calling us 'Little Botanists' and we value this nickname. But it would be very erroneous to assume that everything in us is little. We may be little in stature and significance, but on the other hand, a big Russian heart beats in us...\" (OR IRLI RAN, f. 462, Archive of M.A. Beketova No. 129, sheet 1)."
msgstr "Ivan Romazanovich Tarkhanov (1846—1908) — Petersburg physiologist, student of I.M. Sechenov."
msgstr "Valerian Viktorovich Polovtsov (1862—1918) — plant physiologist and biologist-methodologist. Graduated from Petersburg University (1888), worked in the botanical laboratory of A.S. Famintsyn, Master of Botany (1902), professor of Novorossiysk University (1909), professor of the Geneva Pedagogical Institute in Petersburg (1915). For more details see: B.E. Raikov \\[18\\]."
msgstr "The courses were, like the laboratory, a private educational institution, first with a two-year, and then with a three-year course of study. In addition to pedagogy, natural and mathematical sciences, their program included humanities disciplines — history, philosophy, history of literature and psychology. From 1905, the courses received the name Free Higher School — the first public university with a four-year course of study in three departments (natural, historical and physical), from 1912 — P.F. Lesgaft Higher Courses, and from 1918 (after the closure of the natural and historical departments) — P.F. Lesgaft Institute of Physical Education (now the P.F. Lesgaft State Institute of Physical Culture). On the basis of the Biological Laboratory in 1918, the P.F. Lesgaft Petrograd Scientific Institute was organized, which was then transformed into the P.F. Lesgaft State Natural Science Institute (for more details see Yakovlev N.N., 1990)."
msgstr "In turn, some critical remarks regarding the assessments of B.E. Raikov and A.A. Shcherbakova see in the book by P.A. Henkel \"Khristofor Yakovlevich Gobi\" (M.: Nauka, 1976)."
msgstr "Abroad, this work became known from the annotation given by Tsvet in one of the German journals (Tswett M., 1902, No. 24)"
msgstr "Nikolai Vasilyevich Sorokin (1846—1909) — agro-soil scientist and mycologist.
